Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.

So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (i.e., not parallel to x+y=0). Actually line x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines are lines that are not parallel to any of these three lines. So they cannot have slope 0 (horizontal), infinite slope (vertical), or slope -1. We need to determine all nonnegative integers k (depending on n) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering the specified points.

We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1, and exactly k of those lines are sunny. We'll attempt to construct for various k, and derive necessary conditions.

Observation: The points lie in a triangular grid. There are three families of lines that are "axis-aligned" in a sense: horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), and slope -1 (x+y = constant). These are not sunny. They can cover many points. But we also have sunny lines, which can be any lines with slope not 0,∞,-1.

We need to cover all points. Let's denote T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ | a+b ≤ n+1}. The size is N = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. A line can cover at most something like the number of points on it (within T_n). Since the lines are arbitrary, we can choose lines that pass through many points. The maximum number of points that a line can contain from T_n depends on the slope. For a line with rational slope, it can intersect the triangular region in a set of lattice points. We need to see if it's possible to cover all points with n lines. For large n, we can use n vertical lines (x = 1,...,n) covering all points. So k=0 is always possible. Similarly, n horizontal lines, n lines of slope -1, all non-sunny. So 0 is always possible.

Now, can we have k=n? That would be all lines sunny. Is it possible? Let's try to construct n sunny lines covering T_n. Since we have n lines, we can try to use lines of slope 1 (y = x + c). But those lines are sunny. However how many points of T_n can a line of slope 1 pass through? For a given c, the line y = x + c intersects the triangular region. Points (a,b) with b = a + c, a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 => a+(a+c) ≤ n+1 => 2a + c ≤ n+1. Since a≥1. Also b≥1 => a+c ≥ 1. So a must satisfy 1 ≤ a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/2). The number of integer points on such a line within T_n is about n/2. So with n such lines, we could cover many points. But can we cover all points? The points have various differences b - a. For each point (a,b), the difference d = b - a ranges from -(n-1) to n-1 (since a,b up to n). For a point with difference d, the line y = x + d passes through it. So if we include lines for all d from -(n-1) to n-1, that's 2n-1 lines. But we have only n lines. So we need to select a subset of those lines (or use other sunny lines) such that all points are covered. For example, maybe we can use lines of slope 2 and slope 1/2 etc. But we need to cover all points. Let's think about the covering problem more generally.

We have n lines. Since the points are many, we need each line to cover many points on average. The maximum coverage per line is something like the maximum number of lattice points in T_n that lie on a line. For a line with slope p/q in lowest terms, the line passes through lattice points at intervals. The number of such points in a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between lattice points along the line). For a line with slope 1, the spacing is sqrt(2). The number of points is about (n^2/2) / (something)? Actually we need to compute maximum possible number of points on a line within T_n. Since T_n is a triangular array of size n, the maximum number of points on a line is something like n (if the line is horizontal or vertical or slope -1, those have many points). Indeed, for a horizontal line y = b (b≥1), the points with that y-coordinate are (a,b) with a≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1 - b. So there are n+1 - b points. The maximum occurs when b=1, giving n points. Similarly vertical lines x = a give n points. For slope -1 lines x+y = c, the points with that sum are (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b = c, so there are c-1 points, max when c = n+1 giving n points. So non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. Sunny lines, if they have rational slope, might also cover many points, but perhaps not as many as n? Let's examine.

Consider a line with slope 2 (y = 2x + c). For integer points, x must be such that y is integer. The spacing between integer points on such a line is sqrt(1^2+2^2) = sqrt(5) if slope 2. But the number of lattice points on the line within T_n is at most something like floor((n+1)/something). Let's compute. For a given c, the line passes through points (x, 2x+c) with x integer. The condition a,b≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1 => x ≥ 1, 2x+c ≥ 1, and x+(2x+c) = 3x + c ≤ n+1. So x ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/3). The number of integer x satisfying is roughly (n)/3. So at most about n/3 points. For slope 1/2, similarly about n/3. For slope 1, about n/2. For slope 2, about n/3. So the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover is something like floor((n+1)/2) (if slope 1), but is it possible to cover n points? For slope 1, if c = 0, the line y = x passes through (1,1),(2,2),...,(n,n). But those points have a+b ≤ 2n, but we only have a+b ≤ n+1. So points with a=b≥? For a=b, a+b = 2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of points is about n/2. So indeed a sunny line of slope 1 can cover at most about n/2 points. Similarly, slope 2 gives about n/3. So sunny lines cover fewer points on average than non-sunny lines which can cover up to n points. But we have n lines total. The covering condition is that all points are covered by at least one line. Since we can use non-sunny lines to cover many points, we can achieve covering with many non-sunny lines. But we want to have exactly k sunny lines, and the rest non-sunny. So we can design such that the non-sunny lines cover most points, and the sunny lines cover some remaining points. However we have exactly n lines. The number of non-sunny lines is n - k. So we need to cover N points with n - k non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines can each cover up to n points, we can cover N points with, say, n lines all non-sunny. But if we include sunny lines, we need to compensate by having non-sunny lines cover more points, but we have fewer non-sunny lines. The challenge is to see which k are possible.

We need to determine all k for which there exists a configuration. Let's explore small n to guess pattern. Then try to prove necessary conditions. Then construct for all possible k. n≥3. Let's compute for n=3, we found possible k: 0,1,3. For n=4, let's try to see possibilities.

n=4. Points: (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 5. So points: a=1,b=1..4; a=2,b=1..3; a=3,b=1..2; a=4,b=1. Total 10 points. Need 4 lines covering them. We want to see which k are possible.

We can attempt to construct with k=0: all lines non-sunny. That's easy: take 4 vertical lines x=1,2,3,4. That covers all points. So k=0 possible.

k=4: all lines sunny. Is that possible? We need 4 sunny lines covering all 10 points. Each sunny line can cover at most about n/2 = 2 points? Actually for n=4, slope 1 lines: y = x + c. For c=1, points: (1,2),(2,3) (2,3 sum=5), (3,4) sum=7 >5, so 2 points. For c=2, (1,3),(2,4) (2,4 sum=6>5), so only (1,3). That's 1 point. For c=-1, (2,1),(3,2) (3,2 sum=5) -> 2 points. We already have k=0 always possible. k=n (all sunny) seems unlikely for n≥3 except perhaps small n like n=3 (we found it's possible). The number of lattice points on a line y = mx + b within T_n is at most something like floor((n+1)/something). We need to compute the maximum possible number of points a line (not parallel to axes or slope -1) can contain from T_n. Let's denote the line L. For it to contain many points, it must have rational slope with small denominator. But also the line must pass through points with integer coordinates. If the slope is p/q in lowest terms, then the line can contain integer points spaced by (q,p) (i.e., (Δx, Δy) = (q,p)). So the points are of the form (x0 + kq, y0 + kp). So the number of such points within a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between points along the line). For a given slope, the spacing between lattice points is sqrt(q^2+p^2). But the number of lattice points on the line within T_n is limited by the extent of T_n. Since T_n is a triangle of size n (in terms of coordinates), the maximum number of lattice points on a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 could be something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1, floor((n+1)/3) for slope 2, etc. So the maximum is achieved at slope 1 (or maybe slope 1/2, etc., but slope 1 gives the largest count). But is there a sunny line that can contain n points? For slope 1, the line y = x + c passes through points where a+b = 2a + c (since b = a + c). The condition a+b ≤ n+1 gives 2a + c ≤ n+1. The number of integer a≥1 such that this holds is floor((n+1 - c)/2). The maximum of this over c is when c is as small as possible, but c must be such that b = a + c ≥ 1. If c is negative, b might be less than 1 for small a. For example, c = - (n-1) gives b = a - (n-1). For a = n, b = 1, so point (n,1) is included; but also for a = n-1, b = 0 (not allowed). So the number of points on such a line is something like floor((n+1 - c)/2) for c up to n-1, but also need to consider that b must be ≥1. The maximum count is when c = 0, giving points (1,1),(2,2),...,(floor((n+1)/2), floor((n+1)/2)). That's about n/2. So indeed a sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points. For n even, that's n/2. For n odd, (n+1)/2. So that's about n/2. So with n sunny lines, max coverage is n * (n/2) = n^2/2, but these lines may overlap. But we have N = n(n+1)/2 points. n * (n/2) = n^2/2, while N is about n^2/2 + n/2. So the difference is n/2. So with all sunny lines, we could potentially cover all points if we choose lines cleverly (maybe not all slope 1). However we have only n lines, and each line covers at most ~n/2 points. The total coverage sum (distinct points) can be up to n * (n/2) = n^2/2, but we need N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So we need to cover n/2 more points than the maximum sum of covered points if lines are disjoint. But if lines overlap, the union size is smaller. So to achieve N, we need that the sum of the sizes of the point sets of the lines (with multiplicity) is at least N, but since lines can share points, the union size is ≤ sum. So we need sum ≥ N. But sum of sizes is at most n * (max per line). With all sunny lines, max per line is about n/2. So sum ≤ n * (n/2) = n^2/2. That's less than N for n≥3. Therefore it's impossible to cover all N points with n sunny lines. Indeed N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2, while n * (n/2) = n^2/2. The difference is n/2. So with n sunny lines, the total maximum number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most n^2/2, but we need to cover N points, which is larger. However is it possible that a sunny line covers more than n/2 points? Let's compute for slope 1, we got about n/2. Could there be a sunny line that covers, say, n points? For example, a line with slope 2? That's at most about n/3. Slope 1/2 at most about n/3. So indeed slope 1 gives the maximum. So the maximum number of lattice points a line can contain from T_n is at most floor((n+1)/2) for slopes not 0, ∞, -1? But we need to also consider lines that are not rational slope? But is it possible that a sunny line with slope 1 (or -1? but slope -1 is not sunny) passes through more points than floor((n+1)/2) if we consider lines that are not necessarily passing through lattice points but still cover lattice points? But we are counting lattice points on the line; if the line has slope 1 and passes through (1,1) and (2,2), etc., the number of integer points on it within T_n is as computed. However could a line with slope 1 pass through, say, (1,2) and (2,3) etc., but that's also slope 1. The number of points with b = a + 1 is about n/2 as well. So indeed floor((n+1)/2). So that's the maximum. So for n sunny lines, sum ≤ n * floor((n+1)/2). That's less than N for n≥3. Therefore k=n is impossible for n≥3 (except maybe n=3 where floor((3+1)/2)=2, n * 2 = 6, N=6, so equality holds, thus possible. For n=3, we indeed constructed with 3 sunny lines covering all points. That's a special case because N = 6, n * max_sunny = 3 * 2 = 6. For n=4, n * max_sunny = 4 * 2 = 8 < 10. So k=n impossible for n≥4. So k can be at most n, but with n≥4, k=n is impossible. For n=3, k=n is possible. So the answer likely depends on n.

The maximum union from two non-sunny lines is 7. The next possible union sizes? Let's list all possible unions from two lines (any types). Since families have different point counts. Thus all pairs that cover 6 points (intersection type) fail. What about pairs that cover 5 points or less? Then complement size > 4, so we would need to cover more than 4 points with 2 sunny lines. But 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points (if each covers 2 distinct points). So if complement size > 4, impossible. Therefore for n=4, k=2 impossible. So far we have evidence that k=2 impossible for n=4. But we need to be careful: could we have a configuration where the 2 sunny lines cover points that are not disjoint from the non-sunny lines, but the union is all points, but the complement (points not covered by non-sunny lines) is larger than 4, but the sunny lines cover some of those points and also some of the already covered points, so they can cover more points? Actually the covering condition is that each point is on at least one line. The union of all lines must be all points. If we have 2 non-sunny lines covering some set S, and 2 sunny lines covering some set T, then the union is S ∪ T. The complement (points not in S) is U \ S. For points in U \ S to be covered, they must be in T. So T must contain U \ S. So |T| ≥ |U \ S|. T is the set of points on the sunny lines. But the sunny lines are lines; they may contain points that are also in S. So T can be any subset of points that lie on those lines. The maximum size of T (distinct points) is at most sum of the point counts of the sunny lines (since they are lines). But we can choose the sunny lines arbitrarily. So to cover all points, we need that the union of the lines (all four) is T_n. The sunny lines contribute some points. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines such that their union is T_n. The complement size (points not on the non-sunny lines) must be covered by the sunny lines. If the complement size is 5 or more, then the sunny lines would need to cover at least 5 points. But with 2 sunny lines, the maximum distinct points they can cover is at most something like: if each sunny line can contain at most 2 points (as argued), then max is 4. So if complement size ≥5, impossible. So we need complement size ≤4. Complement size = |T_n| - |S|, where S is the set of points covered by non-sunny lines. |S| can be at most 7. So complement size is at least |T_n| - 7 = 3. So complement size can be 3,4,5,6,... up to 10. For complement size 3, we saw that covering with 2 sunny lines is impossible because 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but they need to cover 3 points. However we also need to see whether 2 sunny lines can cover a set of 3 points that are not collinear? In principle, if the 3 points are such that two of them are on a sunny line and the third is also on a sunny line (maybe same line), then we could cover them with 2 sunny lines. But we saw that in the examples with complement size 3, the points are such that any pair is not sunny, so two sunny lines would need to cover at least 2 points each? Actually with 2 sunny lines, to cover 3 points, we could have one sunny line covering 2 points, and another sunny line covering the remaining 1 point. That's possible if the pair is sunny. But we observed that the complements of max-union pairs (size 3) are sets of three points where no pair is sunny. So they cannot be covered. But could we have a different pair of non-sunny lines that cover 7 points but with a different complement of size 3 that has a sunny pair? Let's enumerate all pairs of non-sunny lines that achieve 7 points. The ones we listed: H1&H2, V1&V2, D5&D4, H1&V1, H1&D5, V1&D5. Also possibly H2&H3? H2 covers 3, H3 covers 2, sum 5, not max. So those are the ones with max 7. For n=4, the complement of H1&H2 is (1,3),(1,4),(2,3) (size 3). That's not sunny pair. Similarly V1&V2 complement (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's also not sunny pair. D5&D4 complement (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's not sunny pair. H1&V1 complement (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's not sunny pair. H1&D5 complement (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). That's not sunny pair. V1&D5 complement (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's not sunny pair. So all complements of size 3 have no sunny pair. Therefore covering with 2 sunny lines impossible. So for n=4, k=2 impossible.

Now, could k=0,1,3 be possible? We already have examples. k=0 with vertical lines. k=1 with e.g., H1 and V1 (two non-sunny) and one sunny covering the missing point? But we need exactly k sunny lines. For n=4, k=1 means one sunny line and three non-sunny lines. Can we cover T_4 with three non-sunny lines and one sunny line? Yes, we gave an example with three horizontals y=1,y=2,y=3 covering 4+3+2=9 points, leaving (1,4) uncovered. Then one sunny line through (1,4) (any line) covers it. But is that sunny? A line passing through (1,4) can be chosen to be sunny, e.g., y = x + 3 (slope 1) passes through (1,4) because 4 = 1+3. That line is sunny. So that's a valid configuration. So k=1 possible. Similarly, we can use three verticals covering 9 points, leaving (4,1) uncovered. That's also covered by a sunny line. So k=1 works. For k=3, we already gave an example with three sunny lines (H1? Actually we used H1 as non-sunny? Wait for n=4, we gave an example with three sunny lines and one non-sunny (y=1) covering the bottom points. That's k=3. So indeed 0,1,3 are possible for n=4.

Now, what about k=2 for n=4? We determined impossible. So the possible k for n=4 are 0,1,3. That's similar to n=3 where possible k are 0,1,3 (but note for n=3, we also had k=3 possible, and k=1 possible). For n=3, we had k=0,1,3. So for n=3 and n=4, the possible k are the same: 0,1,3. That's interesting. But we need to consider n≥3. Let's test n=5 to see pattern.

n=5. Points: (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. So total N = 5*6/2 = 15. We need 5 lines. We can try to construct for various k. Likely some k are impossible. Let's attempt to derive necessary conditions.

We have three families of non-sunny lines. A line can be sunny or not. We want to find all k such that there exists a set of n distinct lines covering all points with exactly k sunny lines.

Observation: The points are in a triangular grid. There is a known technique: each line (whether sunny or not) can cover at most some number of points. For non-sunny lines, max is n. For sunny lines, max is floor((n+1)/2). But is it possible that a sunny line covers more than floor((n+1)/2) points? For n=5, floor((5+1)/2)=3. So a sunny line can cover at most 3 points. Let's compute. Slope 1 lines: y = x + c. Points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. For c=0, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum=6 ≤6, so included. Also (4,4) sum=8 >6, so only up to (3,3). That's 3 points. For c=1, (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) (3,4 sum=7>6), so (3,4) out, (2,3) sum=5, (1,2) sum=3, so 2 points? Actually (2,3) is allowed, (3,4) not allowed. So 2 points. So max is 3. Could a sunny line with slope 2 cover 3 points? y = 2x + c. For n=5, T_5: a+b ≤6. For c=0, points: (1,2) sum3, (2,4) sum6, (3,6) out, so (1,2),(2,4) that's 2 points. For c=-1, (1,1) sum2, (2,3) sum5, (3,5) sum8 out, so 2 points. So at most 2. So indeed max for sunny is floor((n+1)/2) for n odd gives (n+1)/2 = 3 for n=5, that's 3. So that's the maximum.

Now, suppose we have k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines. The total maximum number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most k * M_sunny + (n-k) * M_nonsunny, where M_nonsunny = n (since non-sunny can cover up to n points). But this is an upper bound on the sum of sizes of point sets of the lines. However we need to cover N points. Since lines may overlap, the union size ≤ sum. So to cover N points, we need sum ≥ N. So a necessary condition is:

k * L_s + (n - k) * n ≥ N, where L_s is max points per sunny line (which is floor((n+1)/2)). But is it necessary that the sum of point counts (each line's intersection with T_n) is at least N? Yes, because union ≤ sum, so if sum < N, then union < N, cannot cover all points. So we need sum ≥ N. This is a necessary condition. However it's not sufficient; we also need that the lines are distinct and the covering condition can be satisfied with that sum. But we can try to use this condition to bound k.

Compute N = n(n+1)/2. L_s = floor((n+1)/2). For n even, n=2m, then L_s = (2m+1)/2 = m + 0.5, floor = m. For n odd, n=2m+1, L_s = (2m+2)/2 = m+1. So L_s = ceil(n/2) maybe? Let's compute: n=3 => L_s = 2 = ceil(3/2)=2. n=4 => L_s = 2 = ceil(4/2)=2. n=5 => L_s = 3 = ceil(5/2)=3. So L_s = ceil(n/2). Indeed floor((n+1)/2) = ceil(n/2). So L_s = ceil(n/2).

Now, necessary condition: k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: k * ceil(n/2) + n^2 - k n ≥ n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2.

So n^2 + k (ceil(n/2) - n) ≥ (n^2 + n)/2.

Compute ceil(n/2) - n. For n=2m: ceil(2m/2) = m, then m - 2m = -m = -n/2. For n=2m+1: ceil((2m+1)/2) = m+1, then (m+1) - (2m+1) = -m = -(n-1)/2. So in both cases, ceil(n/2) - n = -floor(n/2) maybe? Actually n even: ceil(n/2) - n = n/2 - n = -n/2 = -floor(n/2). n odd: ceil(n/2) - n = (n+1)/2 - n = (n+1-2n)/2 = (1-n)/2 = -(n-1)/2 = -floor(n/2). Since floor(n/2) = m for n=2m or 2m+1. So ceil(n/2) - n = -floor(n/2). So the inequality becomes:

n^2 - k * floor(n/2) ≥ (n^2 + n)/2 → n^2 - (n^2 + n)/2 ≥ k * floor(n/2) → (2n^2 - (n^2 + n))/2 = (n^2 - n)/2 ≥ k * floor(n/2). So:

k ≤ (n^2 - n) / (2 floor(n/2)).

Now compute for n even: n=2m, floor(n/2) = m. Then (n^2 - n) = 4m^2 - 2m = 2m(2m - 1). Divide by 2m → (2m(2m-1)) / (2m) = (2m-1) = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd: n=2m+1, floor(n/2) = m. Then (n^2 - n) = (4m^2+4m+1) - (2m+1) = 4m^2+4m+1-2m-1 = 4m^2+2m = 2m(2m+1) = 2m(n). Actually n=2m+1, so 2m(2m+1) = 2m n. Then (n^2 - n) / (2 floor(n/2)) = (2m n) / (2m) = n. So k ≤ n. That seems to suggest that for odd n, k can be up to n. But we already saw for n=3, k=n=3 is possible, and k=2 is impossible. So the necessary condition is not sufficient. The inequality is just a bound. For odd n, it doesn't forbid k=n. For even n, it says k ≤ n-1, so k=n is impossible. That's consistent: for n=4, k=4 impossible; for n=6, k=6 impossible. So we have that.

Now, we also need to consider a stronger necessary condition: Since the non-sunny lines are limited in direction, maybe we can derive restrictions on the number of sunny lines that can be included while still covering all points. For example, we saw for n=3, k=2 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible. That suggests that for n≥3, k cannot be n-1? Let's check: for n=3, n-1=2 impossible. For n=4, n-1=3 is possible (we found an example). So it's not that. For n=5, n-1=4; is k=4 possible? Need to examine. So the pattern is not simply about parity.

Let's try to construct for n=5, k=4. That would be 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line can cover up to 5 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points. So the maximum total points covered (with multiplicity) is 5*3=15. So to cover all 15 points, the sunny lines must have disjoint point sets (since sum of point counts equals N). That would require that each sunny line covers exactly 3 points, and the sets of points on the lines are pairwise disjoint and together cover all points. Moreover, the lines must be distinct. So we need to find 5 lines, each sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1), each containing 3 points from T_5, and those 5 sets of 3 points partition T_5. Is that possible? Let's explore.

For a line to contain 3 lattice points from T_n, it must have rational slope with small denominator. The number of lattice points on the line within a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between lattice points). For a line with slope p/q in lowest terms, the lattice points are spaced by (q,p). The number of such points in a triangle of size n is at most something like floor((n+1)/something). But we need to compute the maximum possible number of points a sunny line can contain from T_n. Let's do a more systematic analysis.

Define T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. For a line L, let P(L) = L ∩ T_n. We want to know max |P(L)| when L is not parallel to axes or to x+y=0. The problem is to "Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny." So the answer is a set (maybe depending on n). We need to provide a rigorous solution.

Thus the solution set might be {0,1,3} for n≥3, with some special cases maybe n=3 has k=3 possible, n=4 has k=3 possible, n=5 has k=3 possible. So all n≥3, k=3 possible. But we also need to consider k= n (all sunny) is possible only for n=3. For n=4,5,... it's impossible. So the answer depends on n. The problem states "Let n ≥ 3 be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to find, for each n≥3, the set of k. So we need to produce a characterization.

Let's try to derive necessary conditions.

Define T_n as before. We have n lines L_1,...,L_n, some are sunny, some are not. The covering condition: T_n ⊆ ∪ L_i. Also lines are distinct.

We can try to bound the number of sunny lines. Intuitively, non-sunny lines cover many points, but they are limited in direction. Perhaps we can argue that if there are at least 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered (union) is at most something, but we need to cover N points. Let's denote a_i = |L_i ∩ T_n|. Then ∑ a_i ≥ N. For non-sunny lines, a_i ≤ n. For sunny lines, a_i ≤ ceil(n/2). But we can also get stronger: a_i ≤ something like the maximum size of a line's intersection with T_n given its direction. For non-sunny lines, they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For these directions, the maximum is n (achieved by lines with the smallest sum). So we have a_i ≤ n.

Now, suppose we have k sunny lines. Then ∑ a_i ≤ k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n. This is an upper bound on sum. Since ∑ a_i ≥ N, we get:

k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n ≥ N. (1)

This gives a necessary condition. Let's compute (1) for n:

N = n(n+1)/2.

ceil(n/2) = ⌈n/2⌉.

So (1) becomes:

k⌈n/2⌉ + n^2 - k n ≥ n(n+1)/2 → n^2 + k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ (n^2+n)/2 → k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ (n^2+n)/2 - n^2 = (n^2+n - 2n^2)/2 = (-n^2 + n)/2 = -(n^2 - n)/2.

Thus k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ -(n^2 - n)/2.

Now ⌈n/2⌉ - n is negative. Multiply both sides by -1, reversing inequality:

k(n - ⌈n/2⌉) ≤ (n^2 - n)/2.

But n - ⌈n/2⌉ = ⌊n/2⌋. So we have:

k⌊n/2⌋ ≤ (n^2 - n)/2.

Thus k ≤ (n^2 - n) / (2⌊n/2⌋).

Now compute this bound. For n even, n=2m: ⌊n/2⌋ = m. Then (n^2 - n) = 4m^2 - 2m = 2m(2m-1). Dividing by 2m gives (2m-1) = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd, n=2m+1: ⌊n/2⌋ = m. Then (n^2 - n) = (4m^2+4m+1) - (2m+1) = 4m^2+2m = 2m(2m+1) = 2m n. Then (2m n) / (2m) = n. So k ≤ n. So the bound gives:

- If n is even: k ≤ n-1.
- If n is odd: k ≤ n.

So k=n is not ruled out for odd n, but we need to check if k=n is actually attainable for odd n≥3. For n=3, it is attainable. For n=5, we argued it's not attainable because the maximum total points from 5 sunny lines is at most 11, but we need 15. So the bound from sum is not sufficient; we need a stronger bound based on the structure of sunny lines. So we need to examine the maximum possible ∑ a_i for k sunny lines, but we used the worst-case upper bound a_i ≤ ceil(n/2). However that's an upper bound; the sum may be lower than that. The inequality (1) is a necessary condition; it doesn't guarantee that the sum of a_i can achieve that bound. For k=n, we need ∑ a_i ≥ N. If the maximum possible ∑ a_i for n sunny lines is less than N, then k=n is impossible. So we need to compute the maximum possible sum of |L_i ∩ T_n| over n distinct lines that are all sunny. That's a combinatorial optimization: we have n lines, each sunny, and they must be distinct. The maximum total number of points (counting multiplicities) is the sum of sizes of intersections, but we are not constrained to have them disjoint; we can have lines overlapping. The sum is maximized when each line covers as many points as possible. But there might be limitations because if two sunny lines intersect at a point, they may share points, but that doesn't reduce the sum (it's counted multiple times). So to maximize sum, we want each line to have as many points as possible, regardless of overlap. However we must also consider that the lines are distinct. So we can simply take, for each possible slope that yields many points, a line. But we have n lines; we can choose them to be, say, all of slope 1, but with different intercepts. However only one of those lines (c=0) yields ceil(n/2) points; others yield fewer. So to maximize sum, we might also include lines with other slopes that can also have many points. But we need to examine the maximum possible sum of |L_i ∩ T_n| for n distinct sunny lines.

But the covering condition requires that the union covers all points. If we have n sunny lines, the maximum possible union size (distinct points) is at most ∑ a_i, but could be less due to overlap. However the necessary condition we derived (∑ a_i ≥ N) is a bound on sum. If ∑ a_i < N, then impossible. So we need to see whether it's possible for n sunny lines to have ∑ a_i ≥ N. That's a necessary condition. For n odd, we have k ≤ n, which doesn't forbid k=n. But we need to see if ∑ a_i can be ≥ N. For n=5, we computed max ∑ a_i for 5 sunny lines is 3+2*4=11, which is <15. So k=5 impossible. So the necessary condition from sum bound is not enough to rule out k=n for odd n>3. So we need a more refined necessary condition.

We can try to find an upper bound on ∑ a_i for sunny lines based on the geometry. Perhaps we can show that any sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points, and also that if we have many sunny lines, they must cover many points, but they cannot cover all points because the points are in a triangular arrangement and sunny lines are "thin". Maybe we can prove that if k ≥ 2, then the total number of points on sunny lines is at most something like (n choose 2) etc. But we need a rigorous proof.

Let's think about the structure of T_n. Points are lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. There are three families of lines that are "axis-aligned" (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). Sunny lines are those not in these families. They can have any slope not 0, ∞, -1. But note that a line with slope 1 (sunny) can pass through many points, but there are also lines with slope 2, etc. The maximum number of points a line can contain from T_n is something like max( n, floor((n+1)/2) ) but for sunny it's floor((n+1)/2). For n large, that's about n/2. So each sunny line covers at most about n/2 points. So with n sunny lines, the maximum total sum (over all lines) is at most n * floor((n+1)/2) ≈ n^2/2. That's less than N ≈ n^2/2 + n/2. So the sum bound alone doesn't rule out k=n for odd n, because the bound from sum gave k ≤ n, but the maximum sum for k=n is at most n * ceil(n/2). For n odd, ceil(n/2) = (n+1)/2. Then sum ≤ n*(n+1)/2 = N. So it's possible that ∑ a_i = N. However we need to check if we can achieve ∑ a_i = N with n sunny lines. That would require that each sunny line covers exactly ceil(n/2) points and that the sets of points on each line are pairwise disjoint (since sum of sizes equals N). But is it possible to have n distinct sunny lines each containing ceil(n/2) points, and those points partition T_n? Let's examine for n=3: ceil(3/2)=2, n=3, N=6, we achieved that with 3 sunny lines each covering 2 points, and they partition T_3 (since each pair is sunny and disjoint). For n=5, ceil(5/2)=3, N=15, we would need 5 sunny lines each covering 3 points, partition T_5. But we argued that only one line (y=x) can cover 3 points, others at most 2. So impossible. So the impossibility for odd n≥5 is due to the fact that there is a limited number of lines that can achieve the maximum, and the maximum is not attained by many lines. So we need to characterize which k are possible.

Let's think about the geometry. The points are arranged in a triangular lattice. There are three families of lines that can cover many points: horizontal, vertical, and slope -1. Let's try to derive necessary conditions by double counting or using the fact that each line can cover at most a certain number of points. For a given configuration of n lines (distinct) covering T_n, let L_s be the set of sunny lines (size k), L_ns be the non-sunny lines (size n - k). Then the total number of points counted with multiplicity (sum of |L_i ∩ T_n|) is at least N. So we have:

∑_{L∈L_ns} |L∩T_n| + ∑_{L∈L_s} |L∩T_n| ≥ N.

Now, for each non-sunny line, |L∩T_n| ≤ n. For each sunny line, |L∩T_n| ≤ ceil(n/2). This gives an upper bound on the sum. So we get:

(n - k) n + k ceil(n/2) ≥ N. That's the bound we already have. This bound is not sufficient to rule out k=2 for n=3, because it's satisfied. Approach: Suppose we have a configuration with exactly k sunny lines. Then the set of lines that are not sunny are of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Let's denote H, V, D. The max coverage of m non-sunny lines is achieved by taking m lines of the same type (horizontal or vertical) that are as "low" as possible. The maximum number of distinct points covered by m non-sunny lines is:

- For horizontals: sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1 - i) = m n - m(m+1)/2? Actually H_b for b=1..m cover points with y≤m. The count is (n) + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1) = m n - m(m-1)/2? For n large, that's about n^2 - 2n - (n^2/2 - ...) = about n^2/2 + something. But we need to compute N - coverage. However the max coverage from m non-sunny lines might be limited because they cannot be all parallel? They can be parallel. So we can take m horizontals (or verticals). That's allowed. So the maximum distinct points covered by m non-sunny lines is the maximum over possible families of the union size. But we can also mix families to get larger union? For example, taking a horizontal and a vertical intersect, so union size is sum - 1. To maximize union, we want lines to be disjoint (parallel). So the best is to take m lines of the same type. So that's the max.

Thus with m = n - 2 non-sunny lines, the maximum possible union size is:

U_max = sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1 - i) = m n - m(m-1)/2. (since for i from 1 to m, the y-coordinate = i gives count n+1-i). For m = n-2, we get:

U_max = (n-2)n - (n-2)(n-3)/2.

Simplify: (n-2)n = n^2 - 2n. (n-2)(n-3)/2 = (n^2 -5n +6)/2. So:

U_max = n^2 - 2n - (n^2 -5n +6)/2 = (2n^2 -4n - n^2 +5n -6)/2 = (n^2 + n -6)/2 = (n^2 + n -6)/2.

Now, N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So N - U_max = (n^2 + n)/2 - (n^2 + n -6)/2 = 6/2 = 3. So indeed, with n-2 non-sunny lines, the best we can do (max coverage) is to cover N - 3 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. That's the minimal possible complement size. Let's attempt a rigorous proof for k=2.

We have T_n. Suppose we have n lines L_1,...,L_n, distinct, covering T_n. Exactly k of them are sunny. We want to determine for which k this is possible. The problem asks to "determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions". So we need to produce a characterization. We can propose that the possible k are 0,1,3. And we need to prove that for any n≥3, k=2 and k≥4 are impossible. But note that for n=3, k≥4 impossible because k≤3. So we can handle n≥4 for k≥4. For n=3, we need to show that k=2 impossible. That's part of the proof. So overall:

- For n≥3, k=0,1,3 are possible.

Now, the harder part is proving impossibility for k=2 and k≥4. Let's attempt a general proof.

We have T_n. Suppose we have n lines L_1,...,L_n, with exactly k sunny lines. The other n-k lines are not sunny, so each is parallel to either x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. Let's denote the set of lines as L = L_sunny ∪ L_nonsunny.

Observation: The points in T_n can be partitioned into three "families" of lines of slopes 0, ∞, -1, each covering many points. However, maybe we can consider the points with a = b (the diagonal). Those points are (1,1),(2,2),..., up to (⌊(n+1)/2⌋, ⌊(n+1)/2⌋). There are ⌈n/2⌉ such points. The line y = x is sunny and covers them. Similarly, the line y = x - 1 is sunny and covers some points. But we need a contradiction for k=2.

Let's attempt to bound the number of points that can be covered by two sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most ceil(n/2) points, and there are two sunny lines, the total number of points counted with multiplicity (sum of sizes) is ≤ 2 ceil(n/2). However, the union of these two lines can be at most that. But we need to cover N points. The non-sunny lines can cover many points, but maybe they cannot cover all points because they are limited in direction. For instance, consider the points that are not on any of the non-sunny lines. If we have only two sunny lines, then we have n-2 non-sunny lines. We can try to analyze the possible coverage of the non-sunny lines. The maximum coverage of n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. So there are at least 3 points not covered by the non-sunny lines. Those 3 points must be covered by the two sunny lines. So we need to show that any two sunny lines cannot cover a set of 3 points that are all in T_n and also that those 3 points are such that any two of them are not on a sunny line. But is it forced that the complement (uncovered by non-sunny lines) must contain a triple with no sunny pair? That seems plausible because the complement is small (size 3) and its structure is determined by which non-sunny lines we choose. However, we can choose the non-sunny lines arbitrarily. So to prove impossibility for k=2, we assume there exists a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny. Let S be the set of points covered by those n-2 lines. Then T_n \ S must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. So we need to show that no matter how we choose the n-2 non-sunny lines, the uncovered set has size at least 3, and moreover, it cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any such set of points (of size at least 3) cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But is that true? Let's test with n=4, we had non-sunny lines: two horizontals covering 7 points, leaving 3 points. Those 3 points are (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). No pair is sunny. So cannot cover with 2 sunny lines. Could we choose a different set of 2 non-sunny lines (since n-2 = 2 for n=4) that covers, say, 8 points? But max is 7. So any set of 2 non-sunny lines covers at most 7 points, leaving at least 3 points. Those at least 3 points might be such that some pair is sunny. But we need to examine if there exists a selection of 2 non-sunny lines that leaves a complement of size 3 where some pair is sunny. For n=4, the best coverage is 7, leaving 3 points. The complement set is determined by which lines we choose. We can choose two horizontals y=1 and y=2, covering 4+3=7 points, complement = points with y≥3: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). That's size 3. If we choose two verticals x=1 and x=2, complement = points with x≥3: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's also size 3. If we choose one horizontal and one vertical, coverage is 4+4-1=7, complement = points with a≥2,b≥2? Actually for n=4, H1 (y=1) covers 4, V1 (x=1) covers 4, union 7, complement = points with a≥2,b≥2 and a+b≤5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) – that's size 3. So all complements of size 3 (the minimal) are such that any two points are not on a sunny line. So that's impossible.

Now, for n=5, we have n-2 = 3 non-sunny lines. The max coverage is 12, leaving 3 points. The complement is size 3. Can we find a set of 3 non-sunny lines that cover 12 points and leave a complement of size 3 where some pair is sunny? The complement of three horizontals (y=1,2,3) is size 3, but those points are (1,4),(1,5),(2,4). That's not in sunny pairs. Could we cover 12 points with a mix of horizontals and verticals? For example, take H1 (y=1) 5, H2 (y=2) 4, and V1 (x=1) 5, but H1∪H2∪V1 union size is 5+4+5 - overlaps (H1∩V1 = (1,1), H2∩V1 = (1,2), H1∩H2 = ∅, H2∩V1 = (1,2) already, H1∩V1 = (1,1)). So union = 5+4+5 - 2 (since (1,1) and (1,2) are in two lines) = 12. That's also 12. Complement size 3. The complement would be points not on any of those lines. Those would be points with y≥3 and x≥2 maybe. Let's list all points for n=5 and see which are covered by H1,H2,V1. That might be different. But we need to prove impossibility for any possible set of n-2 non-sunny lines. The approach might be: Show that any covering with exactly 2 sunny lines would force that the set of points not covered by the non-sunny lines has size at least 3, and also that these points must contain a pair that is not on a sunny line (or all pairs are not sunny). But perhaps a stronger result: In any covering of T_n with n lines, the number of sunny lines is at most 3. That would cover both k=2 and k≥4. Let's test with n=3, we have 3 sunny lines, so ≤3. For n=4, we can have 3 sunny lines. For n=5, we can have 3 sunny lines, but we cannot have 4 or 5. So indeed, maybe the maximum number of sunny lines is 3 for n≥3. That seems plausible. If we can prove that, then the possible k are 0,1,2,3? But we already suspect k=2 impossible, so max is 3. So we need to prove that if there are 4 or more sunny lines, it's impossible. And also prove that if there are 2 sunny lines, it's impossible. So we need to show that any covering with n lines must have at most 1 sunny line? But we have an example with 3 sunny lines. So that's not true. So we need a more subtle argument.

Let's try to prove: For n≥3, any covering of T_n with n distinct lines must have at most 3 sunny lines. That's a statement. We can try to prove that if there are at least 4 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered by those sunny lines (union) is at most something less than N, but the non-sunny lines are few, so they cannot cover the rest. However, we need to consider that the sunny lines can overlap, so their union might be smaller than the sum. But we can bound the union of k sunny lines by something like N - (something). Let's explore.

First, note that any sunny line L can intersect T_n in a set of points that is a subset of T_n. The maximum size is ceil(n/2). So with k sunny lines, the union size ≤ k * ceil(n/2). That's a bound. For n≥4, k≥4, we have k * ceil(n/2) ≥ 4 * ceil(n/2). For n even, ceil(n/2)=n/2, so k * n/2 ≥ 2n. For n odd, ceil(n/2)=(n+1)/2, so k * (n+1)/2 ≥ 4*(n+1)/2 = 2(n+1). That's > N? N = n(n+1)/2. For n large, 2n is much smaller than n^2/2. So that bound doesn't help. So we need a different bound.

We need to consider that the sunny lines cannot cover many points because they are limited in direction, but also they cannot cover points that are on anti-diagonals? Actually they can cover some points. The issue is that if we have many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, so they cannot cover the many points that are not on sunny lines. But we need to quantify the maximum possible union of any set of k sunny lines within T_n. It might be that the union of k sunny lines cannot exceed something like (n choose 2) or something, but that seems unlikely. Let's think about the geometry.

T_n is a set of integer points. Consider the three directions of non-sunny lines. The points can be grouped into rows, columns, and anti-diagonals. A sunny line, unless it is parallel to one of those, can intersect each row and column in at most one point. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each row (horizontal) and at most one from each column (vertical). Since T_n has n rows (y=1..n) and n columns (x=1..n). So a sunny line can contain at most n points (if it is a horizontal line) but for sunny lines, it's less. Actually a sunny line cannot be horizontal or vertical, so it is not parallel to axes. So it intersects each row (horizontal) in at most one point. Since there are n rows, the maximum number of points it can have is n, but is it possible to have n points? That would require that the line passes through all n rows, each intersection point is a lattice point. But to have n lattice points on a line, the line must have slope 0 or ∞, which are not sunny. So for a sunny line, the maximum is less than n. Indeed, we computed max = ceil(n/2). So that's a bound. But that bound is not based on rows/columns; it's based on lattice points within T_n. So we can use that.

Now, if we have k sunny lines, they each have at most ceil(n/2) points, so total points (counted with multiplicity) ≤ k * ceil(n/2). The union is at most that. So to cover N points, we need k * ceil(n/2) ≥ N. That's the necessary condition we already have. That condition doesn't forbid k≥4 for odd n. For n odd, N = n(n+1)/2. ceil(n/2) = (n+1)/2. Then k * (n+1)/2 ≥ n(n+1)/2 ⇒ k ≥ n. So that would require k ≥ n. So for odd n, the necessary condition from sum bound is that k ≥ n if we want to cover all points with sunny lines only. But we also have non-sunny lines. The sum bound is not necessary because we have non-sunny lines that can cover points. The inequality we derived earlier was:

(n - k) n + k ceil(n/2) ≥ N. That's an upper bound on sum of a_i? Actually that's a lower bound on the sum of a_i (since we need ∑ a_i ≥ N). The sum of a_i is ≤ (n - k) n + k ceil(n/2). So we get (n - k) n + k ceil(n/2) ≥ N. That's a necessary condition. For k = n (all sunny), this gives n ceil(n/2) ≥ N. For n odd, n ceil(n/2) = n*(n+1)/2 = N, so equality holds. So it's not ruled out. But the sum bound is not sufficient; we need to see if we can actually achieve that sum with n distinct sunny lines. That is, can we have n sunny lines such that each has ceil(n/2) points and the sets of points are disjoint? That would sum to N. But we suspect it's impossible for n≥4. So we need a stronger necessary condition.

Maybe we can use a double counting argument with "lines" and "points" and also consider that the points can be partitioned into three families of lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal). A sunny line cannot contain more than ceil(n/2) points, but also it cannot contain points from all rows/columns because it's not parallel to axes. Maybe we can use a parity or combinatorial argument based on the fact that T_n is a set of points with a+b ≤ n+1, and that the lines covering it must have certain slopes. Let's try to approach the problem from a different perspective: The problem asks to "determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions". So it's an existence problem. We can try to construct for all k that are possible, and then prove that for other k, it's impossible. The proof of impossibility likely involves an invariant or a counting argument that shows that if exactly k lines are sunny, then the covering condition forces k ≤ 3 and also k ≠ 2? But we have an example with k=3. So we need to show that k=2 and k≥4 are impossible. For k≥4, we can attempt to prove that the total number of points on sunny lines (union) is ≤ something like (k * ceil(n/2)) but that's not strong. However, note that if k ≥ 4, then the number of non-sunny lines is n - k ≤ n - 4. The maximum coverage of those non-sunny lines is something like (n - k) n - (n - k)(n - k - 1)/2. That's N - something. For k large, that something is large, so the complement (points not covered by non-sunny lines) is large. The sunny lines must cover those points. But we have k sunny lines, each can cover at most ceil(n/2) points. So total capacity is k ceil(n/2). For k ≥ 4, can k ceil(n/2) be less than the complement size? Let's compute. The complement size = N - |S|, where |S| is the number of points covered by non-sunny lines. The maximum possible |S| is achieved by taking the non-sunny lines as parallel with maximum counts. That maximum is (n - k) n - (n - k)(n - k - 1)/2. That's N - something. Let's compute N - (max |S|). N = n(n+1)/2. max |S| = (n - k)n - (n - k)(n - k - 1)/2. Then complement size ≥ N - max |S|. But we cannot assume that |S| is max; it could be less, making complement larger. So to get a contradiction, we need to show that for any possible selection of n - k non-sunny lines, the complement size is at least something that exceeds the maximum possible union of k sunny lines. That is, we need a lower bound on complement size. The complement size is at least N - (maximum possible coverage of n - k non-sunny lines). But that's a lower bound? Actually if the coverage is less than maximum, the complement is larger, so the lower bound (worst case) is when coverage is maximum. So we have:

|complement| ≥ N - max_coverage_non_sunny.

Now, max_coverage_non_sunny is achieved by taking those n - k lines as parallel with maximum counts. So we have a lower bound on complement size. Then we can compare with the maximum possible union of k sunny lines. If the lower bound on complement size is greater than the maximum possible union of k sunny lines, then impossible. That would prove that for certain k, covering is impossible. Let's compute.

Let m = n - k. Then max coverage from m non-sunny lines is:

C_max(m) = m n - m(m-1)/2.

Thus complement size ≥ N - C_max(m) = n(n+1)/2 - m n + m(m-1)/2 = (n(n+1) - 2 m n + m(m-1))/2.

Simplify: n(n+1) - 2 m n = n^2 + n - 2 m n = n^2 - 2 m n + n = n^2 - 2 m n + n. Then add m(m-1) = m^2 - m. So numerator: n^2 - 2 m n + n + m^2 - m. That's (n^2 - 2 m n + m^2) + (n - m) = (n - m)^2 + (n - m) = (n - m)(n - m + 1). So complement size ≥ (n - m)(n - m + 1)/2. But n - m = k. So complement size ≥ k(k+1)/2.

That's interesting. So the complement size (points not covered by the non-sunny lines) is at least k(k+1)/2. That's a lower bound. Since the non-sunny lines could cover less, complement larger. So we have:

T_n \ (union of non-sunny lines) has size ≥ k(k+1)/2.

Now, these points must be covered by the sunny lines. So the union of sunny lines must contain at least k(k+1)/2 points. But the union of sunny lines is at most k * ceil(n/2). So we need:

k * ceil(n/2) ≥ k(k+1)/2.

If this inequality fails, then impossible. That's a necessary condition. Let's test for various k.

We have ceil(n/2) = ⌈n/2⌉. For n≥3, this is at least 2. So we need:

k * ⌈n/2⌉ ≥ k(k+1)/2 → ⌈n/2⌉ ≥ (k+1)/2 → 2⌈n/2⌉ ≥ k+1.

Thus k ≤ 2⌈n/2⌉ - 1. For n even, ⌈n/2⌉ = n/2, then 2 * n/2 = n → k ≤ n - 1. For n odd, ⌈n/2⌉ = (n+1)/2, then 2 * (n+1)/2 = n+1 → k ≤ n. So this inequality is satisfied for k ≤ n-1 (even) or k ≤ n (odd). That doesn't forbid k≥4 for n≥4, because n-1 ≥ 4 for n≥5, and n≥4 for odd n. So this bound doesn't rule out.

But we used a lower bound on complement size that is k(k+1)/2. That's based on the maximum coverage of non-sunny lines. That's a lower bound on complement size. So we have |complement| ≥ k(k+1)/2. That's correct. Then we need to see whether the sunny lines can cover that many points. The maximum union of k sunny lines is at most k * ceil(n/2). So we need:

k * ceil(n/2) ≥ k(k+1)/2. That's the necessary condition. For k=2, this gives 2 ceil(n/2) ≥ 3, which holds for n≥3 (since ceil≥2). So not contradictory. So we need a different lower bound on complement size that is larger. The bound we derived is the minimum possible complement size given m = n - k non-sunny lines. But is it always achievable? That is, can we always find a set of m non-sunny lines that achieves the maximum coverage C_max(m)? Yes, by taking m lines of the same type (horizontals or verticals or slope -1) with the largest counts. However, note that those m lines must be distinct, and they are indeed distinct. So it's possible. Therefore, the complement size could be as low as k(k+1)/2. That's the best scenario for covering with sunny lines. So if even that scenario is impossible, then any covering with k sunny lines is impossible. So we need to prove that for k=2, it's impossible to have the union of 2 sunny lines cover at least 3 points? Wait complement size lower bound is k(k+1)/2. For k=2, that's 3. So we need to cover at least 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, the complement in the optimal case (n-2 horizontals) is a set of 3 points with no sunny pair. That's impossible. But is it necessary that the complement is exactly that set? The complement size is at least 3. If the complement size is >3, then it's even harder. So the only chance is if the complement size is exactly 3 and the 3 points are coverable by 2 sunny lines. But we can argue that in any covering with k=2 sunny lines, the non-sunny lines must be such that the complement size is at least 3, and moreover, we can show that the complement must contain a triple of points that are not in any sunny line's intersection? But perhaps we can prove that if the non-sunny lines are not all parallel, then the complement size is larger than 3, which is impossible to cover with 2 sunny lines because even if they could cover many points, they have limited capacity. But we need a contradiction for all possible configurations. Let's attempt to prove that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the union of those sunny lines cannot contain all points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. Perhaps we can use a double counting argument with the three families and also consider the points that are "far" from the origin.

Another idea: Consider the points (1, n) and (n, 1) which are on the anti-diagonal. They are two points. If we have 2 sunny lines, they could cover these two points. But then also we have many other points. The non-sunny lines can cover many. The problem seems challenging. Let's search for known solutions or patterns. This is likely an olympiad problem. We have T_n. Suppose we have n lines L_1,...,L_n covering T_n. Let U be the set of sunny lines (size k). Let V be the set of non-sunny lines (size n - k). Each line in V has slope 0, ∞, or -1. Consider the three families of lines: horizontal (H), vertical (V), and slope -1 (D). For each such line, we can compute the number of points of T_n it contains. Denote:

- For a horizontal line y = b, size = n+1 - b.
- For a vertical line x = a, size = n+1 - a.
- For a line x+y = c, size = c-1.

Now, note that if we have m non-sunny lines, they could be any combination. Let's try to think about the problem more conceptually. We have a triangular grid of points. We want to cover them with n lines. The lines can be any. The condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" restricts which lines can be chosen. The problem asks to determine all possible k. So it's a combinatorial geometry existence question. Usually, such problems are solved by constructing examples for certain k and then proving impossibility for others via an invariant. I recall a problem from some contest (maybe an IMO Shortlist) about "sunny lines" and covering a triangular set of points. The answer was that k can be 0, 1, 3. And the proof for impossibility of k=2 and k≥4 used the idea of counting points on lines of slopes 0, ∞, -1 and using an inequality derived from the fact that each point lies on at most one line from each of these families? Actually consider the three families: horizontal, vertical, and slope -1. Any point (a,b) lies on exactly one horizontal line (y = b), exactly one vertical line (x = a), and exactly one slope -1 line (x + y = a + b). So the point belongs to three specific lines. Now, if we have a set of lines, the number of lines from these families that pass through the point is something. For a given configuration of lines, we can count the total number of incidences (point-line). On the other hand, we can bound the total incidences from above based on how many lines of each type we have. This is reminiscent of double counting with "lines" and "points". Let's try that.

We have n lines, some are sunny, some are not. The non-sunny lines are of three types: H, V, D. For each such line, we can compute the number of points of T_n it contains. The sunny lines contain at most ceil(n/2) points. But we can also consider the total number of incidences I = ∑_{P∈T_n} (number of lines passing through P). Since each point is covered by at least one line, I ≥ N. Also, I ≤ ∑_{L} |L∩T_n|.

Now, we can try to get an upper bound on I that depends on the numbers of lines of each type. But we also have a lower bound on I from covering (I ≥ N). So if we can show that I ≤ something < N, contradiction. That's a typical approach. However, we need to bound I from above using the fact that each line from a given family covers a certain number of points, but we can also consider that sunny lines cover at most ceil(n/2). So I ≤ (h * n + v * n + d * n) + k * ceil(n/2). But we don't know h,v,d; they sum to n - k. So I ≤ (n - k) n + k ceil(n/2). That's the bound we have. For k=2, that's (n-2)n + 2 ceil(n/2). For n=3, that's 1*3 + 2*2 = 3+4=7 ≥6. So not contradictory. So we need a stronger bound. Perhaps we can improve the bound for non-sunny lines because they cannot be all of the same type and also cover many points due to overlaps with sunny lines? That's not about the maximum.

Maybe we can consider that each point lies on exactly one horizontal, one vertical, and one slope -1 line. So if we sum over all lines (whether sunny or not) of the number of points they contain, we count each point multiple times. But we can also consider that if we have a line that is not sunny, it's in one of these families. The total number of points on all lines of a given family is something like ∑_{L∈F} |L∩T_n|. For the horizontal family, the sum over all horizontals that are chosen is at most something. But we can also consider that the sunny lines are not in these families, so they contribute no points to these families. However, the bound we have is already summing over all lines. The inequality we get is necessary, not sufficient. So it's not contradictory.

Thus the impossibility of k=2 is not obvious from simple counting. We need a different invariant.

Let's consider the points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (m,m) on the diagonal, where m = ceil(n/2). There are ceil(n/2) such points. The line y = x is sunny and contains them. The crucial point is that we need to prove impossibility. The approach of considering the anti-diagonal bound and then the triple complement seems promising. Let's formalize:

Suppose there exists a set L of n distinct lines covering T_n, with exactly k sunny lines. We want to determine for which k this is possible. For k = 0,1,3 we have examples. For k = 2 and k ≥ 4, we need to show impossible. Let's try to prove:

- For k ≥ 4, the number of non-sunny lines is n - k ≤ n - 4. The union of those non-sunny lines can cover at most (n - k) * n points, but that's large. However, we can try to bound the union of sunny lines from above. Since each sunny line can contain at most ceil(n/2) points, the union of k sunny lines is at most k * ceil(n/2). That's a bound. The total number of points is N. So we need k * ceil(n/2) ≥ N - (max union of non-sunny lines). That's not directly a contradiction.

But we can use a stronger bound: Since the sunny lines are not parallel to axes or to x+y=0, they can intersect each row and column at most once. However, the points on the anti-diagonal are n. If k is large, say k ≥ 4, then the sunny lines can cover at most 2 anti-diagonal points (since each sunny line can contain at most 1 anti-diagonal point). That's okay. The non-sunny lines (n - k) are few. The anti-diagonal points must be covered. The sunny lines cover at most 2 anti-diagonal points. So at least n - 2 anti-diagonal points must be covered by non-sunny lines. Since each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 anti-diagonal point, we need at least n - 2 non-sunny lines. That is, n - k ≥ n - 2 ⇒ k ≤ 2. So if k ≥ 3, then n - k ≤ n - 3, and we cannot cover all anti-diagonal points with non-sunny lines because they are too few. But we also have sunny lines covering up to 2 anti-diagonal points. So the maximum anti-diagonal points that can be covered by sunny lines is 2. Therefore, the total number of anti-diagonal points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is at most (n - k) (since each covers at most 1). So the total covered anti-diagonal points ≤ (n - k) + 2. But we need to cover all n anti-diagonal points. Therefore, we require (n - k) + 2 ≥ n ⇒ k ≤ 2. So if k ≥ 3, it's impossible. We need to solve a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's restate:

We have n >= 3 integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both:

- For all positive integers a and b with a+b <= n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. (So we have a grid of integer points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b <= n+1; these points must be covered by the n lines. The lines are distinct (presumably not coincident). We want exactly k of these lines to be "sunny". A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (i.e., not parallel to x+y=0). Actually line x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines are lines that are not parallel to any of these three lines. So they cannot have slope 0 (horizontal), infinite slope (vertical), or slope -1. So they must have slope not equal to 0, ∞, -1. (Including lines that are parallel to these three are not sunny). So we need to determine possible k such that we can find n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers, a+b <= n+1, and exactly k of them are sunny. The lines are arbitrary (not necessarily passing through integer points), but they must cover the points. We need to find all k (nonnegative integers) for which such a configuration exists. Since n is given, k is a number between 0 and n inclusive, but not all may be possible.

We need to determine all nonnegative integers k (depending on n) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering the specified points.

Let's denote S = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ | a+b ≤ n+1}. That's a triangular set of integer lattice points. The points are (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n) (since a+b ≤ n+1, with a=1 => b ≤ n). Similarly (2,1),..., (n,1). So total points: sum_{i=1}^{n} i = n(n+1)/2. But note a,b positive integers. So points are like a triangular grid.

We need n lines (distinct) covering all these points. We can choose lines arbitrarily. The lines are distinct. They can be any lines in the plane. The condition that exactly k of them are sunny imposes a restriction.

We need to determine all k such that there exists a set of n lines covering those points and exactly k sunny lines.

Observations: The set of points is finite, and we have n lines. Typically, covering many points with few lines is possible if lines are chosen appropriately. But we also have the sunny condition. The question likely asks: For a given n≥3, determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which there exist n distinct lines satisfying the covering condition and exactly k are sunny.

We need to analyze constraints.

First, consider the requirement that all points (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1 are covered by at least one line. That's a covering problem. The lines are distinct. We can think about lines that are "sunny" (i.e., not parallel to axes or x+y=0) vs. non-sunny (parallel to one of those). Non-sunny lines are either horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), or with slope -1 (x+y = constant). They are not sunny. So we have n lines; among them, exactly k are sunny. So the other n - k lines are not sunny (i.e., are parallel to at least one of those three directions). However note: a line could be parallel to more than one of those directions? For example, a horizontal line is parallel to x-axis; it's not parallel to y-axis (except if it's vertical? Actually horizontal is parallel to x-axis, not parallel to y-axis; it's also not parallel to x+y=0 unless it's also with slope -1, which it isn't). So lines that are not sunny are those that are parallel to at least one of the three forbidden directions. But can a line be parallel to two of them simultaneously? For a line to be parallel to both x-axis and y-axis, it would have to be both horizontal and vertical, impossible. For a line to be parallel to both x-axis and x+y=0, it would have slope 0 and slope -1 simultaneously, impossible. Similarly, vertical and slope -1 impossible. So each non-sunny line is parallel to exactly one of the three directions (or possibly it is exactly one of those lines, but that's okay). So we can categorize non-sunny lines as horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

Thus we have n lines, some are sunny, some are horizontal, some are vertical, some are slope -1. The covering condition must be satisfied.

We need to find for which k such a configuration exists.

We can attempt to construct lines. Since we have n lines, and we need to cover many points. The points are many; but we can cover many points with a single line if the line passes through many of them. For example, a line with slope -1 passing through points where a+b is constant; there are many such points for each constant. For a given constant c, the points (a,b) with a+b = c are covered by the line x+y = c (if that line is one of our lines). However note that the line x+y = c is parallel to x+y=0, so it's not sunny. So if we use such lines, they are non-sunny. Similarly, horizontal lines y = constant cover points with same y coordinate, which are many. Vertical lines x = constant cover many points.

But we need exactly k sunny lines. So we can choose some sunny lines to cover points that are not covered by non-sunny lines. But we have a limited number of lines: n. The points count is n(n+1)/2. For large n, it's about n^2/2. To cover all points with n lines, each line must cover many points, on average about n/2 points. That's possible if lines are chosen appropriately. However we also have the restriction on sunny lines; but we can use non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) to cover many points. The sunny lines are those that are not parallel to those three directions. They can still cover points, but they are less constrained? Actually they can be any lines with slope not 0, ∞, -1. They could be e.g., y = 2x (slope 2), etc. They can pass through many integer points if the slope is rational and the line passes through lattice points. But we don't need the lines to be "nice" in terms of integer points; they just need to cover the points. For example, a line passing through (1,1) and (2,3) has slope 2, and would cover those two points; but to cover many points, we might need lines that pass through many of the triangular points. Perhaps we can design lines that are all of the form x = a (vertical) or y = b (horizontal) or x+y = c (slope -1). That's three families. If we use only those, we have non-sunny lines. But we can also include sunny lines. The question is to determine all k such that there exists a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points. So we need to see which k are attainable, and which are impossible. Likely the answer is that all k from 0 to n are possible, but maybe some restrictions like k cannot be 0 or 1? Let's explore.

We need n lines covering the triangular grid of size n. The points are (a,b) with a,b>=1, a+b <= n+1. The number of points is n(n+1)/2.

If we take all lines of the form x = a for a = 1,...,n (vertical lines). That's n lines. Do they cover all points? For each point (a,b), x-coordinate is a, so it lies on vertical line x = a. So all points are covered. However, are all these lines distinct? Yes. Are they sunny? Vertical lines are parallel to y-axis, so they are not sunny. So if we take n vertical lines, we have 0 sunny lines. That's a valid configuration? Let's denote L as a set of n lines. They are distinct. For each point P = (a,b) in the set, there is at least one line in L passing through P. The lines can cover multiple points. We can try to construct for any k from 0 to n. Let's see.

We have three families of non-sunny lines: H (horizontal), V (vertical), D (slope -1). These are not sunny. We can use any combination of these. We need to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1, and exactly k of those lines are sunny. We'll attempt to construct for various k, and derive necessary conditions.

Observation: The points lie in a triangular grid. There are three families of lines that are "axis-aligned" in a sense: horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), and slope -1 (x+y = constant). These are not sunny. They can cover many points. But we also have sunny lines, which can be any lines with slope not 0,∞,-1.

We need to cover all points. Let's denote T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ | a+b ≤ n+1}. The size is N = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. A line can cover at most something like the number of points on it (within T_n). Since the lines are arbitrary, we can choose lines that pass through many points. The maximum number of points that a line can contain from T_n depends on the slope. For a line with rational slope, it can intersect the triangular region in a set of lattice points. We need to see if it's possible to cover all points with n lines. For large n, we can use n vertical lines (x = 1,...,n) covering all points. So k=0 is always possible. Similarly, n horizontal lines, n lines of slope -1, all non-sunny. So 0 is always possible.

Now, can we have k=n? That would be all lines sunny. Is it possible? Let's try to construct n sunny lines covering T_n. Since we have n lines, we can try to use lines of slope 1 (y = x + c). But those lines are sunny. However how many points of T_n can a line of slope 1 pass through? For a given c, the line y = x + c intersects the triangular region. Points (a,b) with b = a + c, a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 => a+(a+c) ≤ n+1 => 2a + c ≤ n+1. Since a≥1. Also b≥1 => a+c ≥ 1. So a must satisfy 1 ≤ a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/2). The number of integer points on such a line within T_n is about n/2. So with n such lines, we could cover many points. But can we cover all points? The points have various differences b - a. For each point (a,b), the difference d = b - a ranges from -(n-1) to n-1 (since a,b up to n). For a point with difference d, the line y = x + d passes through it. So if we include lines for all d from -(n-1) to n-1, that's 2n-1 lines. But we have only n lines. So we need to select a subset of those lines (or use other sunny lines) such that all points are covered. For example, maybe we can use lines of slope 2 and slope 1/2 etc. But we need to cover all points. Let's think about the covering problem more generally.

We have n lines. Since the points are many, we need each line to cover many points on average. The maximum coverage per line is something like the maximum number of lattice points in T_n that lie on a line. For a line with slope p/q in lowest terms, the line passes through lattice points at intervals. The number of such points in a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between lattice points along the line). For a line with slope 1, the spacing is sqrt(2). The number of points is about (n^2/2) / (something)? Actually we need to compute maximum possible number of points on a line within T_n. Since T_n is a triangular array of size n, the maximum number of points on a line is something like n (if the line is horizontal or vertical or slope -1, those have many points). Indeed, for a horizontal line y = b (b≥1), the points with that y-coordinate are (a,b) with a≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1 => a ≤ n+1 - b. So there are n+1 - b points. The maximum occurs when b=1, giving n points. Similarly vertical lines x = a give n points. For slope -1 lines x+y = c, the points with that sum are (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b = c, so there are c-1 points, max when c = n+1 giving n points. So non-sunny lines can cover up to n points. Sunny lines, if they have rational slope, might also cover many points, but perhaps not as many as n? Let's examine.

Consider a line with slope 2 (y = 2x + c). For integer points, x must be such that y is integer. The spacing between integer points on such a line is sqrt(1^2+2^2) = sqrt(5) if slope 2. Then the 2 sunny lines need to cover the remaining 2 points. But is it possible to have 2 non-sunny lines covering 8 distinct points? Let's see. The maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover is n = 4. But two non-sunny lines could be, say, vertical x=1 and x=2. Vertical x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4) -> 4 points. x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) -> 3 points. Union size is 7 (since (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4) and (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) are distinct, total 7). That's less than 8. Could we choose two non-sunny lines that cover 8 distinct points? For example, horizontal y=1 and y=2 cover (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) and (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) -> union size 4+3=7. Or slope -1 lines: x+y=3 covers (1,2),(2,1); x+y=4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) -> union size 2+3=5. So maximum union size from two non-sunny lines seems to be 7, because the maximum coverage per line is 4, but they cannot be disjoint in the sense of covering disjoint sets because the points are limited. Let's check if we can have two non-sunny lines covering 8 points with disjoint sets. For disjointness, the sets of points on each line must be disjoint. But the triangle has 10 points. If one line covers 4 points, the other line could cover at most 4 points, but if they are disjoint, union size 8. However is it possible that two non-sunny lines (each max 4) are disjoint and each cover 4 points? Let's examine.

Non-sunny lines are those that are parallel to axes or x+y=0. So they are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For horizontal lines, each y = b covers points (a,b) with a from 1 to n+1-b. The number of points is n+1-b. The maximum is n (when b=1). For b=1, points are (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1). That's n points. For n=4, that's 4 points. For b=2, that's 3 points; b=3, 2; b=4,1. So horizontal lines cover at most 4. Similarly vertical lines cover at most 4. Slope -1 lines cover at most n points (when c=n+1). For n=4, c=5 gives points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) -> 4 points. So each non-sunny line can cover 4 points. To have two disjoint lines covering 4 points each, they would need to cover sets that have no common points. But is it possible that a horizontal line and a vertical line are disjoint? They intersect at a point (the crossing). That point belongs to both lines if it lies in T_n. For example, horizontal y=1 and vertical x=1 intersect at (1,1), which is in T_n. So they share that point. Similarly, a horizontal and a slope -1 line intersect. So any two distinct lines among these families will intersect somewhere, and if that intersection point is within T_n, then they share that point. But could they be disjoint? For two lines to be disjoint in terms of the set of points they cover from T_n, they could be parallel (like two horizontals) but they are distinct lines, so they are parallel, then they are disjoint (no common points). Two horizontals are distinct and parallel, they have no common points. So we could take two horizontals, say y=1 and y=2. That covers 4 and 3 points, union size 7. If we take y=1 and y=3, union size 4+2=6. So maximum union size from two horizontals is 7. Similarly two verticals: x=1 (4 points) and x=2 (3 points) union 7. Two slope -1 lines: x+y=4 (4 points) and x+y=3 (2 points) union 6? Actually x+y=4 gives (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) -> 3 points? Wait for n=4, sum up to 5. Let's compute correctly: points with a+b=4 are (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) -> 3 points. a+b=5 are (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) -> 4 points. So the max is 4 for c=5. Two slope -1 lines: c=5 (4 points) and c=4 (3 points) share points? They are distinct and parallel, so they are disjoint (no common points). Union size 4+3=7. So that's also 7. So any two non-sunny lines can cover at most 7 distinct points (if they are of different types but share a point, union size is less; if they are parallel, union size is sum of their point counts, which max is 4+3=7). So with 2 non-sunny lines, the maximum distinct points covered is 7. Then we need to cover the remaining 3 points with 2 sunny lines. But 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each, so they can cover up to 4 points, but if they are disjoint and cover 2 each, they cover 4 distinct points. However we only have 3 points left. That's possible. But the issue is that we need exactly 2 sunny lines, and the other 2 lines are non-sunny. We can choose the non-sunny lines to cover 7 points, and then the 2 sunny lines must cover the remaining 3 points (they might also cover some of the 7, but then we'd have less coverage). But we need to cover all 10 points, so the union of all lines must be all 10. If the 2 non-sunny lines cover 7 points, and the 2 sunny lines cover 3 points (maybe overlapping with some of the 7), then the total distinct points covered could be at most 7 + (something) - overlap. To reach 10, we need the sunny lines to cover at least 3 points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. But if the sunny lines cover some points already covered, they don't contribute new points. So we need the union of the sunny lines to contain at least 3 points outside the non-sunny union. Since the non-sunny union is at most 7, the complement size is at least 3. So we need the sunny lines to cover all those complement points. But they can cover at most 4 points. That's fine. However we must ensure that the sunny lines are distinct and sunny, and that the configuration exists. Let's try to construct for n=4, k=2. For instance, choose two non-sunny lines: say y=1 (horizontal) covers 4 points, and x=1 (vertical) covers 4 points, but they intersect at (1,1), so union size = 4+4-1 = 7. That's good. Then the remaining points (complement) are 10-7 = 3 points. Which points are not covered by y=1 or x=1? Points with a≥2,b≥2? Let's list all points:

(1,1) covered by both.
(1,2) covered by y=1? No, y=1 is (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). So (1,2) is not on y=1, but it's on x=1? Yes, x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). So (1,2) covered by x=1. (1,3) covered by x=1. (1,4) covered by x=1. (2,1) covered by y=1. (2,2) not on y=1 or x=1. (2,3) not on y=1 or x=1. (3,1) covered by y=1. (3,2) not on y=1 or x=1. (4,1) covered by y=1. So the uncovered points are (2,2), (2,3), (3,2). That's 3 points. Now we need to cover these three points with 2 sunny lines. Can we cover them with 2 sunny lines? (2,2) and (3,2) is horizontal, not sunny. (2,3) and (3,2) slope (2-3)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny. So none of these pairs are sunny. But we could use a sunny line that passes through, say, (2,2) and some other point not in the set, covering only (2,2). But earlier we saw that with y=1 and x=1, complement is {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}. None of those pairs are sunny. So that fails. With y=1 and x=2? Let's compute.
