The problem likely expects an answer like: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n except k = n-1? Or something like that. Let's explore further.

We already have k=0 always possible. k=n (all sunny) seems unlikely for n≥3 except perhaps small n like n=3 (we found it's possible). But for n=4, we argued k=4 impossible because max points per sunny line is 2, and with 4 lines we can cover at most 8 points (if disjoint). But could we use sunny lines that are not of slope 1, but maybe some lines that cover more than 2 points? Let's compute maximum number of lattice points in T_n that lie on a line that is sunny. Since sunny lines are not parallel to axes or slope -1. However they could be of slope 2,3,... and they might intersect T_n in many points if the slope is rational with small denominator. The number of lattice points on a line y = mx + b within T_n is at most something like floor((n+1)/something). We need to compute the maximum possible number of points a line (not parallel to axes or slope -1) can contain from T_n. Let's denote the line L. For it to contain many points, it must have rational slope with small denominator. But also the line must pass through points with integer coordinates. If the slope is p/q in lowest terms, then the line can contain integer points spaced by (q,p) (i.e., (Δx, Δy) = (q,p)). So the points are of the form (x0 + kq, y0 + kp). So the number of such points within a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between points along the line). For a given slope, the spacing between lattice points is sqrt(q^2+p^2). But the number of lattice points on the line within T_n is limited by the extent of T_n. Since T_n is a triangle of size n (in terms of coordinates), the maximum number of lattice points on a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 could be something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1, floor((n+1)/3) for slope 2, etc. So the maximum is achieved at slope 1 (or maybe slope 1/2, etc., but slope 1 gives the largest count). But is there a sunny line that can contain n points? For slope 1, the line y = x + c passes through points where a+b = 2a + c (since b = a + c). The condition a+b ≤ n+1 gives 2a + c ≤ n+1. The number of integer a≥1 such that this holds is floor((n+1 - c)/2). The maximum of this over c is when c is as small as possible, but c must be such that b = a + c ≥ 1. If c is negative, b might be less than 1 for small a. For example, c = - (n-1) gives b = a - (n-1). For a = n, b = 1, so point (n,1) is included; but also for a = n-1, b = 0 (not allowed). So the number of points on such a line is something like floor((n+1 - c)/2) for c up to n-1, but also need to consider that b must be ≥1. The maximum count is when c = 0, giving points (1,1),(2,2),...,(floor((n+1)/2), floor((n+1)/2)). That's about n/2. So indeed a sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points. For n even, that's n/2. For n odd, (n+1)/2. So that's about n/2. So with n sunny lines, max coverage is n * (n/2) = n^2/2, but these lines may overlap. But we have N = n(n+1)/2 points. n * (n/2) = n^2/2, while N is about n^2/2 + n/2. So the difference is n/2. So with all sunny lines, we could potentially cover all points if we choose lines cleverly (maybe not all slope 1). However we have only n lines, and each line covers at most ~n/2 points. The total coverage sum (distinct points) can be up to n * (n/2) = n^2/2, but we need N = n(n+1)/2 = n^2/2 + n/2. So we need to cover n/2 more points than the maximum sum of covered points if lines are disjoint. But if lines overlap, the union size is smaller. So to achieve N, we need that the sum of the sizes of the point sets of the lines (with multiplicity) is at least N, but since lines can share points, the union size is ≤ sum. So we need sum ≥ N. But sum of sizes is at most n * (max per line). With all sunny lines, max per line is about n/2. So sum ≤ n * (n/2) = n^2/2. That's less than N for n≥3. Therefore it's impossible to cover all N points with n sunny lines. Indeed N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2, while n * (n/2) = n^2/2. The difference is n/2. So with n sunny lines, the total maximum number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most n^2/2, but we need to cover N points, which is larger. However is it possible that a sunny line covers more than n/2 points? Let's compute for slope 1, we got about n/2. Could there be a sunny line that covers, say, n points? For example, a line with slope 2? That's at most about n/3. Slope 1/2 at most about n/3. So indeed slope 1 gives the maximum. So the maximum number of lattice points a line can contain from T_n is at most floor((n+1)/2) for slopes not 0, ∞, -1? But we need to also consider lines that are not rational slope? They might contain at most 1 lattice point. So overall maximum is max( n, floor((n+1)/2) )? Actually n is achieved by non-sunny lines. So for sunny lines, max is floor((n+1)/2). That seems plausible. But is it possible that a sunny line with slope 1 (or -1? but slope -1 is not sunny) passes through more points than floor((n+1)/2) if we consider lines that are not necessarily passing through lattice points but still cover lattice points? But we are counting lattice points on the line; if the line has slope 1 and passes through (1,1) and (2,2), etc., the number of integer points on it within T_n is as computed. However could a line with slope 1 pass through, say, (1,2) and (2,3) etc., but that's also slope 1. The number of points with b = a + 1 is about n/2 as well. So indeed floor((n+1)/2). So that's the maximum. So for n sunny lines, sum ≤ n * floor((n+1)/2). That's less than N for n≥3. Therefore k=n is impossible for n≥3 (except maybe n=3 where floor((3+1)/2)=2, n * 2 = 6, N=6, so equality holds, thus possible. For n=3, we indeed constructed with 3 sunny lines covering all points. That's a special case because N = 6, n * max_sunny = 3 * 2 = 6. For n=4, n * max_sunny = 4 * 2 = 8 < 10. So k=n impossible for n≥4. So k can be at most n, but with n≥4, k=n is impossible. For n=3, k=n is possible. So the answer likely depends on n.

Union: (1,1) [1], (2,1) [5], (3,1) [8], (4,1) [10], (1,3) [3], (2,2) [6]. So union size 6. Complement: (1,2) [2], (1,4) [4], (2,3) [7], (3,2) [9]. That's 4 points. Now we need to cover these with 2 sunny lines. Let's see if we can find two sunny lines that cover these 4 points. The points are: A(1,2), B(1,4), C(2,3), D(3,2). Compute slopes between pairs:

- A-B: vertical → not sunny.
- A-C: (3-2)/(2-1)=1 → sunny.
- A-D: (2-2)/(3-1)=0 → horizontal → not sunny.
- B-C: (3-4)/(2-1) = -1 → not sunny.
- B-D: (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- C-D: (2-3)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny.

So sunny pairs: (A,C) (slope 1). That's one pair. The remaining points are B and D. Are they collinear with a sunny line? B(1,4) and D(3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny. So cannot cover both with a single sunny line. Could we cover B and D with two separate sunny lines? That would be 2 lines, each covering one point, but then we need to cover C and A with one sunny line (which is possible). So we would have sunny lines: L1 through A and C (sunny), L2 through B (or some line passing through B) and L3 through D? But we only have 2 sunny lines. So we need to cover B and D with the same sunny line, which is not possible. Alternatively, we could cover B with one sunny line and D with another sunny line, but then we need to cover A and C with a sunny line, that's 3 sunny lines. But we only have 2 sunny lines. So this complement cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. However we could also consider using a sunny line that passes through B and some other point not in complement, covering B only; but then we still need to cover D and the other two points. With 2 sunny lines, we could have one line covering A and C, and the other line covering B and D? But B and D are not on a sunny line. So that fails. Could we use a sunny line that passes through B and A (vertical) but that's not sunny. So no.

Thus H_1 & D_4 fails.

Now try H_2 & D_5. H_2: y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). D_5: x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Intersection: (3,2) is in both? H_2 includes (3,2) (since y=2, a=3, sum=5), D_5 includes (3,2). So union: H_2 ∪ D_5 = (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,4),(2,3),(4,1). That's 6 points. Complement: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1),(4,? Actually (4,1) covered, (3,1) is not covered, (2,1) not covered, (1,1) not covered, (1,3) not covered. Also (2,? (2,1) not covered, (3,1) not covered. So complement: (1,1),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1). That's 4 points. Now check sunny pairs among these:

- (1,1)-(1,3): vertical → not sunny.
- (1,1)-(2,1): horizontal → not sunny.
- (1,1)-(3,1): horizontal → not sunny.
- (1,3)-(2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2 → sunny? slope -2 is not 0,∞,-1, so it's sunny.
- (1,3)-(3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- (2,1)-(3,1): horizontal → not sunny.

So sunny pair: (1,3)-(2,1) (slope -2). That's one pair. The remaining points are (1,1) and (3,1). Are they on a sunny line together? (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal → not sunny. Could we cover (1,1) and (3,1) with the same sunny line? No. So again need to cover 4 points with 2 sunny lines. We have one sunny pair covering (1,3),(2,1). The other two points (1,1),(3,1) cannot be covered by a single sunny line. So fails.

Now try V_1 & D_4. V_1 (x=1) covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). D_4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Intersection: (1,3) is in both. Union: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(3,1) → 6 points. Complement: (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 points. Check sunny pairs among these: (2,1)-(2,3) vertical → not sunny; (2,1)-(3,2) slope (2-1)/(3-2)=1 → sunny; (2,1)-(4,1) horizontal → not sunny; (2,3)-(3,2) slope (2-3)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny; (2,3)-(4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny; (3,2)-(4,1) slope (1-2)/(4-3) = -1 → not sunny. So sunny pair: (2,1)-(3,2). That's one. Remaining (2,3),(4,1). Are they on a sunny line? (2,3)-(4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny. So fails.

Now try V_2 & D_5. V_2 (x=2) covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3). D_5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Intersection: (2,3). Union: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(1,4),(3,2),(4,1) → 6 points. Complement: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(3,1). That's 4 points. Sunny pairs among these: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical; (1,1)-(1,3) vertical; (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (1,2)-(1,3) vertical; (1,2)-(3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2 → sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny. So sunny pair: (1,2)-(3,1). That's one. Remaining (1,1),(1,3). Those are vertical, not sunny. So fails.

Thus all pairs that cover 6 points (intersection type) fail. What about pairs that cover 5 points or less? Then complement size > 4, so we would need to cover more than 4 points with 2 sunny lines. But 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points (if each covers 2 distinct points). So if complement size > 4, impossible. Therefore for n=4, k=2 impossible. So far we have evidence that k=2 impossible for n=4. But we need to be careful: could we have a configuration where the 2 sunny lines cover points that are not disjoint from the non-sunny lines, but the union is all points, but the complement (points not covered by non-sunny lines) is larger than 4, but the sunny lines cover some of those points and also some of the already covered points, so they can cover more points? Actually the covering condition is that each point is on at least one line. The union of all lines must be all points. If we have 2 non-sunny lines covering some set S, and 2 sunny lines covering some set T, then the union is S ∪ T. The complement (points not in S) is U \ S. For points in U \ S to be covered, they must be in T. So T must contain U \ S. So |T| ≥ |U \ S|. T is the set of points on the sunny lines. But the sunny lines are lines; they may contain points that are also in S. So T can be any subset of points that lie on those lines. The maximum size of T (distinct points) is at most sum of the point counts of the sunny lines (since they are lines). But we can choose the sunny lines arbitrarily. So to cover all points, we need that the union of the lines (all four) is T_n. The sunny lines contribute some points. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines such that their union is T_n. The complement size (points not on the non-sunny lines) must be covered by the sunny lines. If the complement size is 5 or more, then the sunny lines would need to cover at least 5 points. But with 2 sunny lines, the maximum distinct points they can cover is at most something like: if each sunny line can contain at most 2 points (as argued), then max is 4. So if complement size ≥5, impossible. So we need complement size ≤4. Complement size = |T_n| - |S|, where S is the set of points covered by non-sunny lines. |S| can be at most 7. So complement size is at least |T_n| - 7 = 3. So complement size can be 3,4,5,6,... up to 10. For complement size 3, we saw that covering with 2 sunny lines is impossible because 2 sunny lines can cover at most 4 points, but they need to cover 3 points. However we also need to see whether 2 sunny lines can cover a set of 3 points that are not collinear? In principle, if the 3 points are such that two of them are on a sunny line and the third is also on a sunny line (maybe same line), then we could cover them with 2 sunny lines. But we saw that in the examples with complement size 3, the points are such that any pair is not sunny, so two sunny lines would need to cover at least 2 points each? Actually with 2 sunny lines, to cover 3 points, we could have one sunny line covering 2 points, and another sunny line covering the remaining 1 point. That's possible if the pair is sunny. But we observed that the complements of max-union pairs (size 3) are sets of three points where no pair is sunny. So they cannot be covered. But could we have a different pair of non-sunny lines that cover 7 points but with a different complement of size 3 that has a sunny pair? Let's enumerate all pairs of non-sunny lines that achieve 7 points. The ones we listed: H1&H2, V1&V2, D5&D4, H1&V1, H1&D5, V1&D5. Also possibly H2&H3? H2 covers 3, H3 covers 2, sum 5, not max. So those are the ones with max 7. For n=4, the complement of H1&H2 is (1,3),(1,4),(2,3) (size 3). That's not sunny pair. Similarly V1&V2 complement (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's also not sunny pair. D5&D4 complement (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's not sunny pair. H1&V1 complement (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's not sunny pair. H1&D5 complement (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). That's not sunny pair. V1&D5 complement (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's not sunny pair. So all complements of size 3 have no sunny pair. Therefore covering with 2 sunny lines impossible. So for n=4, k=2 impossible.

Now, could k=0,1,3 be possible? We already have examples. k=0 with vertical lines. k=1 with e.g., H1 and V1 (two non-sunny) and one sunny covering the missing point? But we need exactly k sunny lines. For n=4, k=1 means one sunny line and three non-sunny lines. Can we cover T_4 with three non-sunny lines and one sunny line? Yes, we gave an example with three horizontals y=1,y=2,y=3 covering 4+3+2=9 points, leaving (1,4) uncovered. Then one sunny line through (1,4) (any line) covers it. But is that sunny? A line passing through (1,4) can be chosen to be sunny, e.g., y = x + 3 (slope 1) passes through (1,4) because 4 = 1+3. That line is sunny. So that's a valid configuration. So k=1 possible. Similarly, we can use three verticals covering 9 points, leaving (4,1) uncovered. That's also covered by a sunny line. So k=1 works. For k=3, we already gave an example with three sunny lines (H1? Actually we used H1 as non-sunny? Wait for n=4, we gave an example with three sunny lines and one non-sunny (y=1) covering the bottom points. That's k=3. So indeed 0,1,3 are possible for n=4.

Now, what about k=2 for n=4? We determined impossible. So the possible k for n=4 are 0,1,3. That's similar to n=3 where possible k are 0,1,3 (but note for n=3, we also had k=3 possible, and k=1 possible). For n=3, we had k=0,1,3. So for n=3 and n=4, the possible k are the same: 0,1,3. That's interesting. But we need to consider n≥3. Let's test n=5 to see pattern.

n=5. Points: (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. So total N = 5*6/2 = 15. We need 5 lines. We can try to construct for various k. Likely some k are impossible. Let's attempt to derive necessary conditions.

We have three families of non-sunny lines. A line can be sunny or not. We want to find all k such that there exists a set of n distinct lines covering all points with exactly k sunny lines.

Observation: The points are in a triangular grid. There is a known technique: each line (whether sunny or not) can cover at most some number of points. For non-sunny lines, max is n. For sunny lines, max is floor((n+1)/2). But is it possible that a sunny line covers more than floor((n+1)/2) points? For n=5, floor((5+1)/2)=3. So a sunny line can cover at most 3 points. Let's compute. Slope 1 lines: y = x + c. Points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. For c=0, points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum=6 ≤6, so included. Also (4,4) sum=8 >6, so only up to (3,3). That's 3 points. For c=1, (1,2),(2,3),(3,4) (3,4 sum=7>6), so (3,4) out, (2,3) sum=5, (1,2) sum=3, so 2 points? Actually (2,3) is allowed, (3,4) not allowed. So 2 points. So max is 3. Could a sunny line with slope 2 cover 3 points? y = 2x + c. For n=5, T_5: a+b ≤6. For c=0, points: (1,2) sum3, (2,4) sum6, (3,6) out, so (1,2),(2,4) that's 2 points. For c=-1, (1,1) sum2, (2,3) sum5, (3,5) sum8 out, so 2 points. So at most 2. So indeed max for sunny is floor((n+1)/2) for n odd gives (n+1)/2 = 3 for n=5, that's 3. So that's the maximum.

Now, suppose we have k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines. The total maximum number of points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most k * M_sunny + (n-k) * M_nonsunny, where M_nonsunny = n (since non-sunny can cover up to n points). But this is an upper bound on the sum of sizes of point sets of the lines. However we need to cover N points. Since lines may overlap, the union size ≤ sum. So to cover N points, we need sum ≥ N. So a necessary condition is:

k * L_s + (n - k) * n ≥ N, where L_s is max points per sunny line (which is floor((n+1)/2)). But is it necessary that the sum of point counts (each line's intersection with T_n) is at least N? Yes, because union ≤ sum, so if sum < N, then union < N, cannot cover all points. So we need sum ≥ N. This is a necessary condition. However it's not sufficient; we also need that the lines are distinct and the covering condition can be satisfied with that sum. But we can try to use this condition to bound k.

Compute N = n(n+1)/2. L_s = floor((n+1)/2). For n even, n=2m, then L_s = (2m+1)/2 = m + 0.5, floor = m. For n odd, n=2m+1, L_s = (2m+2)/2 = m+1. So L_s = ceil(n/2) maybe? Let's compute: n=3 => L_s = 2 = ceil(3/2)=2. n=4 => L_s = 2 = ceil(4/2)=2. n=5 => L_s = 3 = ceil(5/2)=3. So L_s = ceil(n/2). Indeed floor((n+1)/2) = ceil(n/2). So L_s = ceil(n/2).

Now, necessary condition: k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

Simplify: k * ceil(n/2) + n^2 - k n ≥ n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2.

So n^2 + k (ceil(n/2) - n) ≥ (n^2 + n)/2.

Compute ceil(n/2) - n. For n=2m: ceil(2m/2) = m, then m - 2m = -m = -n/2. For n=2m+1: ceil((2m+1)/2) = m+1, then (m+1) - (2m+1) = -m = -(n-1)/2. So in both cases, ceil(n/2) - n = -floor(n/2) maybe? Actually n even: ceil(n/2) - n = n/2 - n = -n/2 = -floor(n/2). n odd: ceil(n/2) - n = (n+1)/2 - n = (n+1-2n)/2 = (1-n)/2 = -(n-1)/2 = -floor(n/2). Since floor(n/2) = m for n=2m or 2m+1. So ceil(n/2) - n = -floor(n/2). So the inequality becomes:

n^2 - k * floor(n/2) ≥ (n^2 + n)/2 → n^2 - (n^2 + n)/2 ≥ k * floor(n/2) → (2n^2 - (n^2 + n))/2 = (n^2 - n)/2 ≥ k * floor(n/2). So:

k ≤ (n^2 - n) / (2 floor(n/2)).

Now compute for n even: n=2m, floor(n/2) = m. Then (n^2 - n) = 4m^2 - 2m = 2m(2m - 1). Divide by 2m → (2m(2m-1)) / (2m) = (2m-1) = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd: n=2m+1, floor(n/2) = m. Then (n^2 - n) = (4m^2+4m+1) - (2m+1) = 4m^2+4m+1-2m-1 = 4m^2+2m = 2m(2m+1) = 2m(n). Actually n=2m+1, so 2m(2m+1) = 2m n. Then (n^2 - n) / (2 floor(n/2)) = (2m n) / (2m) = n. So k ≤ n. That seems to suggest that for odd n, k can be up to n. But we already saw for n=3, k=n=3 is possible, and k=2 is impossible. So the necessary condition is not sufficient. The inequality is just a bound. For odd n, it doesn't forbid k=n. For even n, it says k ≤ n-1, so k=n is impossible. That's consistent: for n=4, k=4 impossible; for n=6, k=6 impossible. So we have that.

Now, we also need to consider a stronger necessary condition: Since the non-sunny lines are limited in direction, maybe we can derive restrictions on the number of sunny lines that can be included while still covering all points. For example, we saw for n=3, k=2 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible. That suggests that for n≥3, k cannot be n-1? Let's check: for n=3, n-1=2 impossible. For n=4, n-1=3 is possible (we found an example). So it's not that. For n=5, n-1=4; is k=4 possible? Need to examine. So the pattern is not simply about parity.

Let's try to construct for n=5, k=4. That would be 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line can cover up to 5 points. The complement (points not covered by that non-sunny line) will be at least N - 5 = 10. With 4 sunny lines, each can cover at most 3 points (since ceil(5/2)=3). So max distinct points covered by 4 sunny lines is at most 4 * 3 = 12, but they may overlap. However we need to cover the complement (10 points) with 4 sunny lines. Could it be done? Possibly, if we choose the non-sunny line appropriately, and then the sunny lines cover the remaining points. But we need to see if it's possible. Let's try to see a pattern. In n=3, we had k=3 possible; k=2 impossible. In n=4, we had k=3 possible; k=2 impossible. So for n=3, the maximum k is 3 (n). For n=4, the maximum k is 3 (n-1). For n=5, perhaps the maximum k is 5? But we need to test. Let's compute N=15. The necessary condition from sum bound gave k ≤ 5. So k=5 is not ruled out. But is it possible? We need to cover 15 points with 5 sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points. So the maximum total points covered (with multiplicity) is 5*3=15. So to cover all 15 points, the sunny lines must have disjoint point sets (since sum of point counts equals N). That would require that each sunny line covers exactly 3 points, and the sets of points on the lines are pairwise disjoint and together cover all points. Moreover, the lines must be distinct. So we need to find 5 lines, each sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1), each containing 3 points from T_5, and those 5 sets of 3 points partition T_5. Is that possible? Let's explore.

T_5 points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. List them:

(1,1) sum2
(1,2) sum3
(1,3) sum4
(1,4) sum5
(1,5) sum6
(2,1) sum3
(2,2) sum4
(2,3) sum5
(2,4) sum6
(3,1) sum4
(3,2) sum5
(3,3) sum6
(4,1) sum5
(4,2) sum6
(5,1) sum6

Total 15.

Now, we need to find 5 lines (sunny) each covering 3 points. Since each sunny line must have slope not 0, ∞, -1. The maximum number of points on a line with slope 1 (sunny) is 3, as we saw. So we could use lines of slope 1 (y = x + c) for various c. For n=5, c=0 gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3). That's 3 points. c=1 gives (1,2),(2,3) (2 points). c=2 gives (1,3),(2,4) (2 points). c=3 gives (1,4),(2,5) (2 points). c=4 gives (1,5) (1 point). c=-1 gives (2,1),(3,2) (2 points). So only c=0 gives 3 points. So we have one sunny line (y=x) covering 3 points. But we need 5 lines. So we need to consider other slopes. For slope 2, maximum points is 2. For slope 1/2, also 2. So it seems that any sunny line can cover at most 3 points, but only slope 1 yields 3 points, and even then only one such line (c=0) yields 3. However there might be other lines with slope not 1 but still contain 3 points if they are not rational slope? But they would contain at most 1 lattice point. So the only way to get 3 points on a sunny line is to have slope 1 and intercept 0. That's the line y=x. So we have only one such line. Therefore with 5 sunny lines, we cannot achieve 5 * 3 = 15 coverage because we can't have 5 lines each covering 3 points; we have at most one line with 3 points. The other sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each. So the maximum sum of point counts from 5 sunny lines is 3 + 2*4 = 11, which is less than 15. Therefore it's impossible to cover all 15 points with 5 sunny lines. So k=5 impossible for n=5. But we need to verify that there isn't some other sunny line with slope something else that can contain 3 points. For n=5, the triangle is of size 5. A line with slope 2 (y=2x) passes through (1,2) (sum3), (2,4) (sum6), (3,6) out, so 2 points. Could a line with slope 2 pass through (2,1) and (3,3) maybe? That's slope 2? Actually (3,3)-(2,1) is (1,2) slope 2, that's not a line of the form y=2x+b. But we can consider any line. For a line to contain 3 lattice points from T_n, it must have rational slope with small denominator. The number of lattice points on the line within a bounded region is roughly (size of region) / (distance between lattice points). For a line with slope p/q in lowest terms, the lattice points are spaced by (q,p). The number of such points in a triangle of size n is at most something like floor((n+1)/something). But we need to compute the maximum possible number of points a sunny line can contain from T_n. Let's do a more systematic analysis.

Define T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. For a line L, let P(L) = L ∩ T_n. We want to know max |P(L)| when L is not parallel to axes or to x+y=0. However note that a line could be parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1) but not sunny; that's excluded. So we consider lines with slope not equal to 0, ∞, -1. The maximum number of lattice points on such a line within T_n is something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1, but for slopes like 1/2, it's about n/3, etc. So indeed the maximum is achieved at slope 1, giving floor((n+1)/2). For n=5, floor((5+1)/2)=3. So that's maximum.

But is it possible that a line with slope 1 (sunny) can contain more than 3 points for n=5? Let's compute precisely. The points on y = x + c with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 6. For c=0, points: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) → 3 points. For c= -1, (2,1),(3,2) → 2 points. So indeed maximum is 3. So at most 1 line (c=0) yields 3. However could there be a line with slope 1 but with a different intercept that also yields 3 points? For c=1, points (1,2),(2,3) → 2. For c=2, (1,3),(2,4) → 2. For c=3, (1,4),(2,5) → 2. For c=4, (1,5) → 1. So only c=0 gives 3. So indeed only one line of slope 1 yields 3.

Now, could there be a line with slope not 1 but still contain 3 points? For example, slope 2 but with intercept such that it goes through (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) but (3,6) is out (sum=9 >6). So 2 points. Could it go through (2,1),(3,3),(4,5) out. So 2. So at most 2. Similarly, slope 3 yields at most 2? Let's check y=3x, (1,3) sum4, (2,6) sum8 >6, so 1 point. So indeed 2 points max for slopes with denominator > 1. But what about slope 1/2? y = (1/2)x, points with x even. For n=5, points (2,1),(4,2) → 2. So 2. So indeed any sunny line can contain at most 3 points, and only slope 1 lines can have 3, but only one such line. So with k sunny lines, the total maximum distinct points they can cover (if they are disjoint) is at most (number of lines with 3) + (others with 2) etc. But we also have non-sunny lines that can cover up to n points each. So we need to design a configuration where the union of all lines covers T_n. The number of sunny lines is k. The rest n-k lines are non-sunny. So we need to see which k are achievable.

The problem is to "Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny." So the answer is a set (maybe depending on n). We need to provide a rigorous solution.

Let's attempt to find a pattern. For n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. For n=4, possible k: 0,1,3. For n=5, we suspect possible k: 0,1, maybe 3, maybe 5? But we need to determine. Let's test n=5 with k=3. Is it possible to have 3 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines covering T_5? That seems plausible. For example, use two non-sunny lines to cover many points, and then 3 sunny lines cover the rest. But we need to see if we can cover all points with 2 non-sunny lines (max union 7 for n=5? Actually n=5, non-sunny max per line is 5. Two non-sunny lines can cover at most 2*5 - overlap. With intersection, max union = 2*5 - 1 = 9. That's less than 15. So complement size = 15 - (union). If we cover 9 points, complement 6. Then we have 3 sunny lines to cover 6 points. That might be possible if the complement can be partitioned into 3 sunny pairs, each pair sunny. But we need to also consider that the sunny lines can cover points that are also covered by non-sunny lines, but they must cover the complement. So we need to find a set of 2 non-sunny lines and 3 sunny lines such that the union is T_5. The complement of the non-sunny lines is a set of points that must be covered by sunny lines. Since we have 3 sunny lines, they can cover at most 3 * 3 = 9 points (if each covers 3 distinct points). But the complement size could be up to 15 - (some coverage). To achieve covering, we need that the complement is subset of union of sunny lines. So we can try to design.

But maybe there is a more systematic approach: the points are in a triangular array. There is a known combinatorial geometry problem about covering lattice points with lines with certain restrictions. The problem asks to determine all k. But we also need to consider k= n (all sunny) is possible only for n=3. For n=4,5,... it's impossible. So the answer depends on n. The problem states "Let n ≥ 3 be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So we need to find, for each n≥3, the set of k. So we need to produce a characterization.

Let's try to derive necessary conditions.

Define T_n as before. We have n lines L_1,...,L_n, some are sunny, some are not. The covering condition: T_n ⊆ ∪ L_i. Also lines are distinct.

We can try to bound the number of sunny lines. Intuitively, non-sunny lines cover many points, but they are limited in direction. Perhaps we can argue that if there are at least 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points covered (union) is at most something, but we need to cover N points. Let's denote a_i = |L_i ∩ T_n|. Then ∑ a_i ≥ N. For non-sunny lines, a_i ≤ n. For sunny lines, a_i ≤ ceil(n/2). But we can also get stronger: a_i ≤ something like the maximum size of a line's intersection with T_n given its direction. For non-sunny lines, they are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For these directions, the maximum is n (achieved by lines with the smallest sum). So we have a_i ≤ n.

Now, suppose we have k sunny lines. Then ∑ a_i ≤ k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n. This is an upper bound on sum. Since ∑ a_i ≥ N, we get:

k * ceil(n/2) + (n - k) * n ≥ N. (1)

This gives a necessary condition. Let's compute (1) for n:

N = n(n+1)/2.

ceil(n/2) = ⌈n/2⌉.

So (1) becomes:

k⌈n/2⌉ + n^2 - k n ≥ n(n+1)/2 → n^2 + k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ (n^2+n)/2 → k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ (n^2+n)/2 - n^2 = (n^2+n - 2n^2)/2 = (-n^2 + n)/2 = -(n^2 - n)/2.

Thus k(⌈n/2⌉ - n) ≥ -(n^2 - n)/2.

Now ⌈n/2⌉ - n is negative. Multiply both sides by -1, reversing inequality:

k(n - ⌈n/2⌉) ≤ (n^2 - n)/2.

But n - ⌈n/2⌉ = ⌊n/2⌋. So we have:

k⌊n/2⌋ ≤ (n^2 - n)/2.

Thus k ≤ (n^2 - n) / (2⌊n/2⌋).

Now compute this bound. For n even, n=2m: ⌊n/2⌋ = m. Then (n^2 - n) = 4m^2 - 2m = 2m(2m-1). Dividing by 2m gives (2m-1) = n-1. So k ≤ n-1. For n odd, n=2m+1: ⌊n/2⌋ = m. Then (n^2 - n) = (4m^2+4m+1) - (2m+1) = 4m^2+2m = 2m(2m+1) = 2m n. Then (2m n) / (2m) = n. So k ≤ n. So the bound gives:

- If n is even: k ≤ n-1.
- If n is odd: k ≤ n.

So k=n is not ruled out for odd n, but we need to check if k=n is actually attainable for odd n≥3. For n=3, it is attainable. For n=5, we argued it's not attainable because the maximum total points from 5 sunny lines is at most 11, but we need 15. So the bound from sum is not sufficient; we need a stronger bound based on the structure of sunny lines. So we need to examine the maximum possible ∑ a_i for k sunny lines, but we used the worst-case upper bound a_i ≤ ceil(n/2). However that's an upper bound; the sum may be lower than that. The inequality (1) is a necessary condition; it doesn't guarantee that the sum of a_i can achieve that bound. For k=n, we need ∑ a_i ≥ N. If the maximum possible ∑ a_i for n sunny lines is less than N, then k=n is impossible. So we need to compute the maximum possible sum of |L_i ∩ T_n| over n distinct lines that are all sunny. That's a combinatorial optimization: we have n lines, each sunny, and they must be distinct. The maximum total number of points (counting multiplicities) is the sum of sizes of intersections, but we are not constrained to have them disjoint; we can have lines overlapping. The sum is maximized when each line covers as many points as possible. But there might be limitations because if two sunny lines intersect at a point, they may share points, but that doesn't reduce the sum (it's counted multiple times). So to maximize sum, we want each line to have as many points as possible, regardless of overlap. However we must also consider that the lines are distinct. So we can simply take, for each possible slope that yields many points, a line. But we have n lines; we can choose them to be, say, all of slope 1, but with different intercepts. However only one of those lines (c=0) yields ceil(n/2) points; others yield fewer. So to maximize sum, we might also include lines with other slopes that can also have many points. But we need to examine the maximum possible sum of |L_i ∩ T_n| for n distinct sunny lines.

But the covering condition requires that the union covers all points. If we have n sunny lines, the maximum possible union size (distinct points) is at most ∑ a_i, but could be less due to overlap. However the necessary condition we derived (∑ a_i ≥ N) is a bound on sum. If ∑ a_i < N, then impossible. So we need to see whether it's possible for n sunny lines to have ∑ a_i ≥ N. That's a necessary condition. For n odd, we have k ≤ n, which doesn't forbid k=n. But we need to see if ∑ a_i can be ≥ N. For n=5, we computed max ∑ a_i for 5 sunny lines is 3+2*4=11, which is <15. So k=5 impossible. So the necessary condition from sum bound is not enough to rule out k=n for odd n>3. So we need a more refined necessary condition.

We can try to find an upper bound on ∑ a_i for sunny lines based on the geometry. Perhaps we can show that any sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points, and also that if we have many sunny lines, they must cover many points, but they cannot cover all points because the points are in a triangular arrangement and sunny lines are "thin". Maybe we can prove that if k ≥ 2, then the total number of points on sunny lines is at most something like (n choose 2) etc. But we need a rigorous proof.

Let's think about the structure of T_n. Points are lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. There are three families of lines that are "axis-aligned" (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). Sunny lines are those not in these families. They can have any slope not 0, ∞, -1. But note that a line with slope 1 (sunny) can pass through many points, but there are also lines with slope 2, etc. The maximum number of points a line can contain from T_n is something like max( n, floor((n+1)/2) ) but for sunny it's floor((n+1)/2). For n large, that's about n/2. So each sunny line covers at most about n/2 points. So with n sunny lines, the maximum total sum (over all lines) is at most n * floor((n+1)/2) ≈ n^2/2. That's less than N ≈ n^2/2 + n/2. So the sum bound alone doesn't rule out k=n for odd n, because the bound from sum gave k ≤ n, but the maximum sum for k=n is at most n * ceil(n/2). For n odd, ceil(n/2) = (n+1)/2. Then sum ≤ n*(n+1)/2 = N. So it's possible that ∑ a_i = N. However we need to check if we can achieve ∑ a_i = N with n sunny lines. That would require that each sunny line covers exactly ceil(n/2) points and that the sets of points on each line are pairwise disjoint (since sum of sizes equals N). But is it possible to have n distinct sunny lines each containing ceil(n/2) points, and those points partition T_n? Let's examine for n=3: ceil(3/2)=2, n=3, N=6, we achieved that with 3 sunny lines each covering 2 points, and they partition T_3 (since each pair is sunny and disjoint). For n=5, ceil(5/2)=3, N=15, we would need 5 sunny lines each covering 3 points, partition T_5. But we argued that only one line (y=x) can cover 3 points, others at most 2. So impossible. So the impossibility for odd n≥5 is due to the fact that there is a limited number of lines that can achieve the maximum, and the maximum is not attained by many lines. So we need to characterize which k are possible.

The problem likely expects an answer like: $k$ is possible iff $k\le n$ and $k\not\equiv 2 \pmod{?}$ No. Let's try to construct for n=6, k=3? Or k=2? Need to see pattern.

Let's compute for n=6. T_6 points: a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 7. Total N = 6*7/2 = 21. Non-sunny max per line is 6. Sunny max per line is floor((6+1)/2) = 3 (since ceil(6/2)=3). Actually let's compute precisely: The set of points on a line with slope 1 (y = x + c) that lie in T_n are those with a≥1, b = a + c ≥ 1, and a + (a + c) = 2a + c ≤ n+1. So a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/2). For c = -1, we get a ≤ floor((n+1 +1)/2?) Actually c = -1 gives b = a - 1, need a ≥ 2, then a ≤ floor((n+1 +1)/2) = floor((n+2)/2). That's ceil((n+1)/2) maybe. But they can cover many, but we need to see if it's possible to cover all points with 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. For n=3, we saw that any pair of non-sunny lines covering 5 points leaves a complement of 1 point, which can be covered by a sunny line, but with 2 sunny lines we would have 1 non-sunny line covering 3 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. Those 3 points are not in sunny pairs. So impossible. For n=4, we had 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could cover up to 7 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. Those 3 points are not in sunny pairs. So impossible. For n=5, we had 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could cover up to 12 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. Those 3 points are not in sunny pairs. So impossible. So pattern suggests that with 2 sunny lines, the complement (points not on the non-sunny lines) will be of size at least something, and those points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because they are all on lines that are not sunny. But we need a proof that for any n≥3, if we have exactly 2 sunny lines, then the covering condition cannot be satisfied. But we also need to consider the possibility that the non-sunny lines are not all of the same type, but they could be chosen to cover many points, leaving a complement that is maybe of size less than something, but we also need to ensure that the 2 sunny lines can cover that complement. The maximum coverage from 2 sunny lines is 2*ceil(n/2) (if they are disjoint). That's at most n (if n even) or n+1 (if n odd). So the complement size must be ≤ that. The complement size is N - |S|, where S is the set of points covered by non-sunny lines. We need to find a configuration where S covers enough points such that N - |S| ≤ 2*ceil(n/2). But also the complement must be coverable by 2 sunny lines, i.e., there exist 2 sunny lines whose union covers that complement. That's a stronger condition. But we can try to prove that for any selection of non-sunny lines (any set of n-2 lines that are not sunny), the complement (points not on those lines) must contain at least 3 points that are all on lines that are not sunny, or something like that. That seems messy.

Alternatively, we can try to bound the total number of points that can be covered by any set of n lines where exactly k are sunny, by considering the number of points on lines of each direction. Perhaps we can use a counting argument with "lines" as sets, but also note that each point lies on some lines. We can try to derive an upper bound on the number of points that can be covered by n lines with exactly k sunny, based on the maximum size of intersections with T_n. Then show that for k≥2, the bound is < N, except when k=3 (and maybe n=3). But we saw that for n=3, k=2 bound is not < N, but still impossible. So we need a more refined bound.

Let's think about the geometry. The points are arranged in a triangular lattice. There are three families of lines that can cover many points: horizontal, vertical, and slope -1. Sunny lines cannot have those slopes. The maximum number of points on a sunny line is ceil(n/2). However, there is also a restriction on how many points a sunny line can contain from T_n if it is not the line y=x. But we can have many sunny lines with different slopes, each covering at most ceil(n/2). But also, if we have many sunny lines, they might be forced to be disjoint in terms of points they cover (or at least their union cannot exceed some bound). However, to cover all N points, we need the union of all lines to be T_n. Since non-sunny lines can cover many points, the sunny lines need to cover the remaining points. So we can think about selecting a set of non-sunny lines that cover as many points as possible, then the sunny lines must cover the complement. The maximum possible size of S (points covered by non-sunny lines) is achieved by taking all non-sunny lines to be of the same type (horizontal or vertical) and as many as possible. But we are free to choose the configuration. So to make k sunny lines possible, we can design the non-sunny lines to cover as many points as possible, thereby reducing the burden on sunny lines. So if we want k sunny lines to be possible, we need to check if there exists a set of n - k non-sunny lines (distinct) that cover at least N - (max points covered by k sunny lines) points, but also the remaining points must be coverable by those k sunny lines. That is a feasibility question.

But perhaps a more systematic approach is to consider the following: For each point (a,b) in T_n, define its "type" in terms of the differences a - b, etc. Maybe we can classify points into lines of slope 1 (y=x) and others. Since sunny lines are not parallel to axes or slope -1, they can be of any slope except 0,∞,-1. The points on a line of slope 1 (y = x + c) are such that a - b = -c. So these are lines with constant difference. The points on a line of slope 2 (y = 2x + c) have a - b = -c? Actually if b = 2a + c, then a - b = a - (2a + c) = -a - c, not constant. So it's not a simple constant. But maybe we can use the fact that any sunny line can contain at most ceil(n/2) points, and also that if we have many sunny lines, they cannot all be of slope 1 because they would intersect. But perhaps we can prove that if there are at least 2 sunny lines, then the total number of points on sunny lines is at most something like (n choose 2) or something? Actually we need a bound on the union size of sunny lines, not sum.

Let's attempt to prove that if k ≥ 2, then the maximum possible union size of k sunny lines within T_n is at most N - something. But we need to cover N points. So maybe we can show that for n≥3, the union of any two sunny lines covers at most N - 3 points? That seems unlikely. For n=3, two sunny lines cover at most 2+2 - overlap? But we already have an example with 3 sunny lines covering all points. Two sunny lines could cover at most 4 points (if disjoint), but N=6, so they cannot cover all points, but we need to cover all points with n lines; for n=3, with k=2, we have 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line covers up to 3 points, but we need to cover the rest. So it's not about union of sunny lines only; it's about the combination.

Thus we need a unified argument.

Let's try to derive necessary conditions by double counting or using the fact that each line can cover at most a certain number of points. For a given configuration of n lines (distinct) covering T_n, let L_s be the set of sunny lines (size k), L_ns be the non-sunny lines (size n - k). Then the total number of points counted with multiplicity (sum of |L_i ∩ T_n|) is at least N. So we have:

∑_{L∈L_ns} |L∩T_n| + ∑_{L∈L_s} |L∩T_n| ≥ N.

Now, for each non-sunny line, |L∩T_n| ≤ n. For each sunny line, |L∩T_n| ≤ ceil(n/2). This gives an upper bound on the sum. So we get:

(n - k) n + k ceil(n/2) ≥ N. That's the bound we already have. This bound is not sufficient to rule out k=2 for n=3, because it's satisfied. But maybe we can improve the bound for sunny lines: For a sunny line, we can bound |L∩T_n| ≤ floor((n+1)/2) as we did. That's same as ceil(n/2). So that's the max. However, we also have the fact that if a sunny line contains ceil(n/2) points, then it must be the line y = x (slope 1) with intercept 0, or perhaps y = x + 1 for n even? Let's compute carefully.

Given n, the maximum number of lattice points in T_n on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 is indeed ⌈n/2⌉. But is it possible that a sunny line with slope not 1 achieves that maximum? For example, slope 1/2 yields at most something like floor((n+1)/3) maybe. So only slope 1 can achieve ⌈n/2⌉. However, we should also consider slopes like 1 (which is sunny). So the maximum is achieved only by lines with slope 1. So any sunny line that achieves the max is of the form y = x + c, and to have ⌈n/2⌉ points, we need c = 0? Let's verify.

We want |L∩T_n| = ⌈n/2⌉. For slope 1, L: y = x + c. The points are (a, a + c) with a≥1, a + c ≥ 1, and 2a + c ≤ n+1. The maximum number of a satisfying is when c is such that the interval for a is as large as possible. For c = 0, a ≤ floor((n+1)/2) → ⌊(n+1)/2⌋ = ⌈n/2⌉. For c = -1, we need a≥2, then a ≤ floor((n+1 + 1)/2) = ⌊(n+2)/2⌋ = ⌈(n+1)/2⌉? Actually (n+2)/2 = n/2 + 1. floor is ⌈n/2⌉ if n even? Let's compute. So indeed, for n even, c=-1 also yields ⌈n/2⌉ points. For n=4, c=-1 gives (2,1),(3,2) → 2 points, but also (1,0) out. So that's also 2. So multiple lines can achieve max. For n=4, the max is 2. Lines y=x (c=0) gives (1,1),(2,2) → 2. y=x-1 (c=-1) gives (2,1),(3,2) → 2. Both are sunny (slope 1). So indeed, there can be multiple sunny lines with max. So the bound from sum doesn't distinguish.

Now, for general n, we need to examine whether there exists a set of n-2 non-sunny lines covering all but at most 2*ceil(n/2) points, and such that the complement can be covered by 2 sunny lines. But the smallest complement size is N - (max coverage of n-2 non-sunny lines). The max coverage of m non-sunny lines is achieved by taking m lines of the same type (horizontal or vertical) that are as "low" as possible. The maximum number of distinct points covered by m non-sunny lines is:

- For horizontals: sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1 - i) = m n - m(m+1)/2? Actually H_b for b=1..m cover points with y≤m. The count is (n) + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1) = m n - m(m-1)/2? Let's compute: sum_{i=0}^{m-1} (n - i) = m n - (m-1)m/2 = m n - m(m-1)/2. That's m n - m(m-1)/2. For m = n-2, that's (n-2)n - (n-2)(n-3)/2. That's something like n(n-2) - (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n large, that's about n^2 - 2n - (n^2/2 - ...) = about n^2/2 + something. But we need to compute N - coverage. However the max coverage from m non-sunny lines might be limited because they cannot be all parallel? They can be parallel. So we can take m horizontals (or verticals). That's allowed. So the maximum distinct points covered by m non-sunny lines is the maximum over possible families of the union size. But we can also mix families to get larger union? For example, taking a horizontal and a vertical intersect, so union size is sum - 1. To maximize union, we want lines to be disjoint (parallel). So the best is to take m lines of the same type. So that's the max.

Thus with m = n - 2 non-sunny lines, the maximum possible union size is:

U_max = sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1 - i) = m n - m(m-1)/2. (since for i from 1 to m, the y-coordinate = i gives count n+1-i). For m = n-2, we get:

U_max = (n-2)n - (n-2)(n-3)/2.

Simplify: (n-2)n = n^2 - 2n. (n-2)(n-3)/2 = (n^2 -5n +6)/2. So:

U_max = n^2 - 2n - (n^2 -5n +6)/2 = (2n^2 -4n - n^2 +5n -6)/2 = (n^2 + n -6)/2 = (n^2 + n -6)/2.

Now, N = n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So N - U_max = (n^2 + n)/2 - (n^2 + n -6)/2 = 6/2 = 3. So indeed, with n-2 non-sunny lines, the best we can do (max coverage) is to cover N - 3 points, leaving 3 points uncovered. That's the minimal possible complement size. Let's try a systematic approach: We have T_n. Consider the set of lines L = L_s ∪ L_ns. Suppose exactly k of them are sunny. Then the other n - k are not sunny. So they have slopes 0, ∞, -1. However, this bound may be too large to be useful. The inequality we get is:

For example, perhaps we can prove that if there are at least 2 sunny lines, then the sum of a_i over sunny lines is at most something like (k * ceil(n/2)) - something, and also that the non-sunny lines cannot cover points that are "far" from the origin due to overlap with sunny lines. This seems messy.

Let's try to think about the problem more conceptually. We have a triangular grid of points. We want to cover them with n lines. The lines can be any. The condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" restricts which lines can be chosen. The problem asks to determine all possible k. So it's a combinatorial geometry existence question. Usually, such problems are solved by constructing examples for certain k and then proving impossibility for others via an invariant. I recall a problem from some contest (maybe an IMO Shortlist) about "sunny lines" and covering a triangular set of points. The answer was that k can be 0, 1, 3. And the proof for impossibility of k=2 and k≥4 used the idea of counting points on lines of slopes 0, ∞, -1 and using an inequality derived from the fact that each point lies on at most one line from each of these families? Actually consider the three families: horizontal, vertical, and slope -1. Any point (a,b) lies on exactly one horizontal line (y = b), exactly one vertical line (x = a), and exactly one slope -1 line (x + y = a + b). So the point belongs to three specific lines. Now, if we have a set of lines, the number of lines from these families that pass through the point is something. For a given configuration of lines, we can count the total number of incidences (point-line). On the other hand, we can bound the total incidences from above based on how many lines of each type we have. This is reminiscent of double counting with "lines" and "points". Let's try that.

We have n lines, some are sunny, some are not. The non-sunny lines are of three types: H, V, D. For each such line, we can compute the number of points of T_n it contains. The sunny lines contain at most ceil(n/2) points. But we can also consider the total number of incidences I = ∑_{P∈T_n} (number of lines passing through P). Since each point is covered by at least one line, I ≥ N. Also, I ≤ ∑_{L} |L∩T_n|.

Now, we can try to get an upper bound on I that depends on the numbers of lines of each type. But we also have a lower bound on I from covering (I ≥ N). So if we can show that I ≤ something < N, contradiction. That's a typical approach. However, we need to bound I from above using the fact that each line from a given family covers a certain number of points, but we can also consider that sunny lines cover at most ceil(n/2). So I ≤ (h * n + v * n + d * n) + k * ceil(n/2). But we don't know h,v,d; they sum to n - k. So I ≤ (n - k) n + k ceil(n/2). That's the bound we have. For k=2, that's (n-2)n + 2 ceil(n/2). For n=3, that's 1*3 + 2*2 = 3+4=7 ≥6. So not contradictory. So we need a stronger bound. Perhaps we can improve the bound for non-sunny lines because they cannot be all of the same type and also cover many points due to overlaps with sunny lines? That's not about the maximum.

Maybe we can consider that each point lies on exactly one horizontal, one vertical, and one slope -1 line. So if we sum over all lines (whether sunny or not) of the number of points they contain, we count each point multiple times. But we can also consider that if we have a line that is not sunny, it's in one of these families. The total number of points on all lines of a given family is something like ∑_{L∈F} |L∩T_n|. For the horizontal family, the sum over all horizontals that are chosen is at most something. But we can also consider that the sunny lines are not in these families, so they contribute no points to these families. However, the bound we have is already summing over all lines. The inequality we get is necessary, not sufficient. So it's not contradictory.

Thus the impossibility of k=2 is not obvious from simple counting. We need a different invariant.

Let's consider the points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (m,m) on the diagonal, where m = ceil(n/2). There are ceil(n/2) such points. The line y = x is sunny and contains them. If we have 2 sunny lines, they could be, say, y = x and y = x + 1, but y = x + 1 passes through (1,2),(2,3), etc. Those points are not on the diagonal. So the diagonal points are only covered by the line y = x. So if we have only 2 sunny lines, and we want to cover all points, then the other n-2 lines must cover the rest. But perhaps we can argue that the diagonal points must be covered by the non-sunny lines because if the sunny lines are not enough to cover them, then the non-sunny lines must cover them. But the non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) cannot cover a point on the diagonal (a,a) unless that line passes through it. Let's check: For a point (a,a), which horizontals pass through it? y = a. That's horizontal. That's non-sunny. Similarly, x = a is vertical. Also, slope -1 lines: x+y = 2a, which is c = 2a. That line is non-sunny. So indeed, the diagonal points are covered by non-sunny lines if those lines are included. So if we have a covering, we could include y = 1,2,...,n-2 as horizontals, and then the diagonal points with a ≤ n-2 are covered by those horizontals? For (a,a) with a ≤ n-2, the horizontal y = a covers it. So they are covered. For a = n-1 (if n-1 ≤ n, but we need a+b ≤ n+1, so (n-1,n-1) sum 2n-2 > n+1 for n≥2, so it's not in T_n). So the diagonal points in T_n are those with a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). That's m = ceil(n/2). So those points are (1,1),(2,2),...,(m,m). They are all covered by horizontals y = 1,2,...,m? For (a,a), y = a is horizontal. If we include horizontals y = 1..(n-2), then those with a ≤ n-2 are covered. But m is about n/2, so m ≤ n-2 for n≥3. So they are covered. So the diagonal points can be covered by horizontals, which are non-sunny. So that's not a problem.

Now, the challenge is to prove impossibility for k=2. Let's try a different approach: Suppose we have a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Then consider the set of points that are not on any of the sunny lines. Those points must be covered by the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are n-2 lines. The total number of points on those lines (union) is at most something, but we can try to show that the sunny lines must be parallel (or something) and that they cannot cover the necessary points. Hmm.

Let's attempt to systematically analyze all possible configurations with k sunny lines, maybe using linear algebra or combinatorial design. Since the problem asks to determine all k, we can try to prove that k must be ≤ 3, and also that k ≠ 2. But we already have an example with k=3. So we need to show that k=2 and k≥4 are impossible. The proof for k≥4 might be easier because the number of sunny lines is large, and the non-sunny lines are few. Then we can try to bound the total number of points that can be covered by the sunny lines (union) from above by something like k * ceil(n/2). That's not strong enough. However, we can also bound the total number of points that can be covered by the non-sunny lines (union) from above by something like (n - k) n. That's large. But we need to show that if k ≥ 4, then the sunny lines cannot cover all points, and the non-sunny lines cannot cover the rest because there are too few non-sunny lines. But we can try to prove that the sum of the maximum possible coverages from sunny and non-sunny lines is less than N, which would be a contradiction. That's the necessary condition we already have. For k=4, we have (n-4)n + 4 ceil(n/2) ≥ N? That's the condition. For n=5, (5-4)*5=5, 4*ceil(2.5)=4*3=12, sum=17, N=15 → satisfied. For n=6, (6-4)*6=12, 4*3=12, sum=24, N=21 → satisfied. For n=7, (7-4)*7=21, 4*4=16, sum=37, N=28 → satisfied. So the necessary condition doesn't forbid k=4. So we need a stronger bound.

But perhaps we can show that if there are k sunny lines, then the total number of points on those lines (counting multiplicities) is at most something like (k * (n - k + 1))? That seems unlikely. Or maybe we can consider that each sunny line cannot contain more than ceil(n/2) points, but also that if there are many sunny lines, they must intersect, and each intersection reduces the capacity. However, we can try to prove that for any set of k sunny lines, the sum of |L_i ∩ T_n| (with multiplicity) is at most something like (k * (n - k + 1))? No.

Let's step back. Maybe we should try to prove that the only possible k are 0, 1, 3 by constructing examples, and then prove that k=2 is impossible by a parity argument using the three families. Consider the points (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n). They lie on the first column. The line x=1 (vertical) is non-sunny. If we have 2 sunny lines, we can cover the first column with some sunny lines and the vertical line. But maybe we can show that the number of points on the first column that are not covered by the non-sunny lines must be at least 2, and those points must be on the sunny lines. But then we can examine the structure of those points.

Let's attempt a proof by contradiction for k=2. Assume there exists a set of n distinct lines L_1,...,L_n covering T_n, with exactly 2 sunny lines. Let the sunny lines be S1 and S2. Let the non-sunny lines be N1,...,N_{n-2}. Each non-sunny line has slope 0, ∞, or -1. Consider the anti-diagonal (x+y = n+1). There are n points. Each non-sunny line covers at most 1 of them. So at most n-2 anti-diagonal points are covered by non-sunny lines. Therefore, at least 2 anti-diagonal points are not covered by any non-sunny line. Those 2 points must be covered by the sunny lines. So S1 and S2 contain those 2 anti-diagonal points. Since S1 and S2 are sunny, they are not parallel to x+y=0, so they intersect that anti-diagonal in at most 1 point each. So they each contain exactly one of those two anti-diagonal points. So we have two distinct anti-diagonal points, say A and B, such that A ∈ S1, B ∈ S2, and they are on the anti-diagonal. That's fine.

Now, also consider the points (1,1) and (n,1) maybe. But perhaps we can get a contradiction by considering the "boundary" of T_n. Another approach: Count the number of points on each of the three "axes" (lines parallel to axes or anti-diagonal). Perhaps we can use the fact that the sum of the numbers of points on all lines of a given type (horizontal) is something, and that each point lies on exactly one such line. If we sum over all chosen horizontals, the total number of incidences with points (i.e., ∑_{L∈H} |L∩T_n|) is equal to ∑_{P∈T_n} (number of chosen horizontals that contain P). Since each point lies on exactly one horizontal line, the number of chosen horizontals containing P is either 0 or 1, depending on whether that horizontal line is selected. So this sum is exactly the number of points that lie on the selected horizontals. That's just the size of the union of those horizontals (since horizontals are disjoint). Similarly for verticals. For slope -1 lines, they are also disjoint (since parallel), so the union size is sum of sizes. So the maximum number of points covered by m non-sunny lines (when they are all parallel) is the sum of their sizes. That's what we used.

Now, suppose we have k=2 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are n-2. Could it be that the non-sunny lines are not all parallel? Then they might have overlaps, reducing the union size. The complement size would be larger. The sunny lines must cover those points. The sunny lines have limited capacity. Maybe we can prove that the total number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is at most something like 2 * ceil(n/2). That's small. The non-sunny lines, even if they are not all parallel, can cover at most (n-2)n points, but they might cover less. But we need a contradiction. Perhaps we can consider that the points on the anti-diagonal (x+y = n+1) are many; if they are not covered by non-sunny lines, they must be covered by sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from that anti-diagonal. That's okay. But perhaps we can also consider the points on the "boundary" with a = 1 or b = 1, etc. The key is to find a set of points that must be covered by the sunny lines, and show that those points are not collinear with a sunny line, and also that the number of such points exceeds the capacity of 2 sunny lines. But we already have an example with n=5 where the complement of 3 horizontals is 3 points, which are not coverable by 2 sunny lines. But that's with n-2 = 3 non-sunny lines. In that case, the complement is exactly 3 points. But we are not forced to have n-2 horizontals. Could we have a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement is, say, 4 points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? For example, take n=4, we have 2 non-sunny lines. If we take 2 verticals, complement is 3 points. If we take 2 horizontals, complement is 3 points. If we take 1 horizontal and 1 vertical, complement is 3 points. So all give complement 3. For n=5, we have 3 non-sunny lines. The maximum coverage is 12, complement 3. If we take 2 horizontals and 1 vertical, coverage is 12, complement 3. If we take 2 horizontals and 1 slope -1 line, coverage is something like 5+4+? Let's compute. 2 horizontals (y=1,2) cover 5+4=9. Add a slope -1 line, say x+y=6 (c=6) covers 5 points. Overlaps? H1∩D: (1,5) is on H1? H1 y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). D c=6 covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Overlap at (5,1) which is on H1? (5,1) has y=1, so H1 covers it. So union size = 9+5-1 = 13. So coverage 13, complement 2. That's smaller complement. But is it possible to have 3 non-sunny lines with complement 2? That would be better for covering with 2 sunny lines. Let's examine n=5, using horizontals y=1,2 and slope -1 line x+y=6. Then union size is 5+4+5-1 = 13 (since (5,1) is overlap). Complement size = 15-13 = 2. So there are only 2 points uncovered. Those two points must be covered by the sunny lines. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover them, perhaps if each sunny line passes through one of them. That seems plausible. So maybe k=2 is possible for n=5 with this configuration? We need to check whether the 2 uncovered points are such that they can be covered by 2 sunny lines. But also, the sunny lines must be distinct and sunny. So we need to find 2 sunny lines (distinct) such that they cover those 2 points. That's easy: we can just take the lines that pass through each of those points and are sunny (e.g., y = x + something). But we also have to ensure that the overall set of n lines (3 non-sunny + 2 sunny) are distinct. That's fine. So this suggests that k=2 might be possible for n=5. Let's test concretely.

But we already saw that the complement size could be as low as 3. Hmm.

Let's try to derive a contradiction using a counting argument with "lines" and "points" but also consider that each sunny line cannot contain more than ceil(n/2) points. Perhaps we can also bound the total number of points that can be covered by the sunny lines from above in a different way that is smaller than the lower bound on complement size derived from the necessary condition. Actually we already derived that complement size ≥ k(k+1)/2. For k=2, that's 3. So we need to cover at least 3 points with 2 sunny lines. That's possible. But we need to see whether the complement can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement is not arbitrary; it's the set of points not on any non-sunny line. But we can also consider that the sunny lines themselves are lines; they can cover points. So if the complement has size 3, we need 2 sunny lines to cover those 3 points. That's possible only if those 3 points are collinear with some sunny line (or each is on a sunny line). The complement size is at least 3. So indeed, in any covering with k=2, the complement (points not on non-sunny lines) has size at least 3, and if it's exactly 3, it's one of those three specific sets. So we need to examine whether any of those three sets can be covered by 2 sunny lines. As we saw, the sets are:

- Complement of 3 horizontals y=1,2,3: points (1,4),(1,5),(2,4) for n=5, but for n=4, complement of 2 horizontals is (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). For n=4, complement of 2 horizontals is size 3. So we need to see if any of those complements can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement of 3 slope -1 lines (c=4,5,6) is (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's size 3. Can 2 sunny lines cover these three points? The line y = x is sunny and contains (1,1) and (2,2) but (2,2) is not in the set. (1,1) is in the set, (1,2) and (2,1) are not on y=x. The line y = -x + 3 is slope -1, not sunny. The line through (1,2) and (2,1) is slope -1, not sunny. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 of them. So impossible. Therefore, if the complement size is 3, covering is impossible.

Now, could it be that the complement size is > 3, and that the sunny lines cover that complement? For example, if complement size is 4, then we need to cover 4 points with 2 sunny lines. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points. So covering 4 points impossible. So this configuration fails. Thus it seems that with k=2, the complement size is at least 3, and in many cases it's larger. But we need a general proof that it's impossible to cover all uncovered points with 2 sunny lines.

Maybe we can prove a stronger statement: For any set of n-2 non-sunny lines (distinct), the complement (points not on them) has size at least 3, and moreover, any sunny line can intersect that complement in at most 1 point. That would imply that with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered. Therefore, if the complement size is at least 3, impossible. I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." This seems like an olympiad problem. The answer is k ∈ {0,1,3}. The proof for impossibility of k=2 and k≥4 might involve an invariant like the sum of the numbers of points on each line of a certain type, and then using the pigeonhole principle to show that the total number of points on sunny lines must be at least something that contradicts the maximum capacity. The sunny lines are not of these types, so they contribute no incidences to these families. So the total incidences I_fam = ∑_{L∈F} |L∩T_n|, where F is the set of chosen lines from the families. This is at most the sum of sizes of selected lines. But we also have the incidences of points with lines from the families that are not selected; they are not counted. So I_fam is the total number of incidences of points with selected lines from families. Since each point lies on exactly one line from each family (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal).
