So many possibilities.

Now, perhaps we can consider the points that are "far" from the origin, like those with a = n or b = n. The anti-diagonal point (n,1) is on the anti-diagonal. Similarly, (1,n). So those are two anti-diagonal points. If they are not covered by non-sunny lines, then they must be covered by sunny lines. So the sunny lines contain those points. That's plausible. But then we need to see whether the complement (points not covered by non-sunny lines) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The complement includes the two anti-diagonal points plus many others. The sunny lines must cover all those. So the sunny lines must contain the two anti-diagonal points. That's okay. But also, they must contain all other points that are not on any non-sunny line. Those points are determined by which non-sunny lines we choose. The problem is that we need to show that no matter which set of n-2 non-sunny lines we pick, the uncovered set cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. That seems like a combinatorial geometry problem. Perhaps we can prove that the union of any two lines (sunny) can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a = 1? Actually we need a more global bound.

Another idea: Use an invariant based on the sum of the coordinates modulo something, like for each point (a,b), consider f(a,b) = a mod 2 or something. The lines that are not sunny have slopes 0, ∞, -1. A line of slope 0 (horizontal) contains points with the same y. If we look at the parity of y, the line contains points with various x, but we can consider something like the sum of the coordinates (a+b) is constant on slope -1 lines. That's a key property. So if we have a line that is not sunny, it is either horizontal (constant y), vertical (constant x), or anti-diagonal (constant x+y). The sunny lines are not of those types. So a sunny line will have varying x, y, x+y. But perhaps we can use the following: For each point P, consider the three values (x, y, x+y). The covering lines are lines. If we have many sunny lines, they must cover many points. But maybe we can prove that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then the number of points on those sunny lines is at most something like 2 * ceil(n/2), but also that the non-sunny lines must cover all other points. However, we can compute a lower bound on the number of points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines based on the fact that the non-sunny lines are limited in number and must cover the anti-diagonal points (n-2 of them). The remaining anti-diagonal points (2) are on sunny lines. The sunny lines also cover some other points. The total number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is at most 2 * ceil(n/2). That's at most n+1 for odd n. For n=5, that's 6. So the sunny lines can cover up to 6 points. The non-sunny lines (3) can cover at most 12 points. So total coverage possible is 18, which is >15. So that's not contradictory. But we need to see if the covering can actually be arranged to cover all 15 points. The constraint is that the non-sunny lines must cover exactly n-2 anti-diagonal points. That means they cover 3 anti-diagonal points, leaving 2 anti-diagonal points on sunny lines. So the sunny lines contain those 2 anti-diagonal points. The sunny lines may also contain some other points. The question is: can we choose 3 non-sunny lines such that they cover all anti-diagonal points except two, and also cover enough other points so that the remaining points (which are not on any non-sunny line) are exactly those two anti-diagonal points and maybe some others, and those are covered by the sunny lines? The sunny lines must cover all uncovered points. So if we can make the uncovered set small (like only 2 points), then the sunny lines can cover them. But we already argued that the complement size is at least 3 because the maximum union of 3 non-sunny lines is 12, so complement ≥3. So the uncovered set has at least 3 points. The sunny lines must cover those at least 3 points. So we need to see whether there exists a set of 3 non-sunny lines such that the uncovered set is exactly 3 points and those 3 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. The uncovered set is the complement of the union. Since the union size is ≤ 12, complement size ≥ 3. To have complement size 3, the union must be exactly 12. That occurs only if the 3 non-sunny lines are all parallel and cover N-3 points. So in that case, the uncovered set is a triple of points. As we have examined, those triples are not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Therefore, if a covering exists, then the non-sunny lines cannot achieve union size 12. So they must achieve union size < 12, i.e., at most 11. Then complement size ≥ 4. So the uncovered set has at least 4 points. Now, the sunny lines must cover those points. But the sunny lines have capacity at most 6 for n=5. So that's not impossible. However, we also have the constraint that the non-sunny lines must cover all but two anti-diagonal points. That's a condition on which anti-diagonal points are covered. If the union size is 11, then the non-sunny lines cover 11 points. Since there are 3 non-sunny lines, and they can cover at most 12 points, it's possible that they cover 11. In that case, the complement has 4 points. Could those 4 points be covered by 2 sunny lines? Possibly, if they are arranged as two pairs on sunny lines. But we need to see if such a configuration can satisfy that the non-sunny lines cover 3 anti-diagonal points (or maybe 2, depending). Let's try to see if we can construct a covering with 2 sunny lines for n=5. We'll try to design. The non-sunny lines are 3. They must cover at least n-2 = 3 anti-diagonal points. So they must cover exactly 3 anti-diagonal points if they cover all but two, or maybe they cover 2 anti-diagonal points and the other 2 are covered by sunny lines, but then the sunny lines cover 2 anti-diagonal points, and the non-sunny lines cover 2 anti-diagonal points? But the sunny lines can cover at most 2 anti-diagonal points because they can cover at most 2 points from the anti-diagonal (each sunny line can cover at most 1). So it's possible that the non-sunny lines cover, say, 1 anti-diagonal point, and the sunny lines cover 2, and the remaining n-3 anti-diagonal points are covered by something else? But we need to cover all n anti-diagonal points. The non-sunny lines are only 3, each can cover at most 1 anti-diagonal point. So the maximum anti-diagonal points that can be covered by non-sunny lines is 3. The sunny lines can cover at most 2. So total possible covered anti-diagonal points is at most 5. That's okay. To cover all 5 anti-diagonal points, we need that the non-sunny lines cover 3 and the sunny lines cover 2, or the non-sunny cover 2 and the sunny cover 3 (but sunny can only cover 2 because there are only 2 sunny lines). Actually sunny lines can cover at most 2 anti-diagonal points (since there are 2). So the maximum anti-diagonal points covered by sunny lines is 2. Therefore, to cover all 5 anti-diagonal points, we need the non-sunny lines to cover at least 3 anti-diagonal points. Since there are 3 non-sunny lines, they can cover at most 3. So it's forced that they cover exactly 3 anti-diagonal points, and the sunny lines cover the remaining 2. So that's necessary. So indeed, in any covering with 2 sunny lines, the non-sunny lines must cover exactly 3 anti-diagonal points, each covering one distinct anti-diagonal point. That means the anti-diagonal points not covered by non-sunny lines are exactly 2, and those must be covered by sunny lines. So that's a necessary condition.

Now, consider the union of the non-sunny lines. The anti-diagonal points they cover are 3. The other points they cover could be many. The complement (uncovered points) includes those 2 anti-diagonal points. Could it be that the complement consists only of those 2 anti-diagonal points? That would mean that all other points are covered by the non-sunny lines. But then the union size would be N - 2. Is that possible with 3 non-sunny lines? The maximum union size is N - 3. So N - 2 > N - 3, so not possible. Therefore, the complement must have at least 3 points. So there is at least one additional point not on any non-sunny line. That point must be covered by the sunny lines. So the sunny lines must contain at least 3 points (the 2 anti-diagonal points plus at least one other). So we have at least 3 uncovered points. That's the lower bound. Now, the sunny lines can cover at most 2 * ceil(n/2) points. For n=5, that's 6. So 3 points is fine. The problem is to prove that no matter how we choose the 3 non-sunny lines that cover 3 anti-diagonal points, the uncovered set (size ≥3) cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we already found an example where the uncovered set is a triple of points that are not on a sunny line, so that fails. But maybe there is a different triple that is coverable? The triple arises from a parallel family of non-sunny lines (horizontals, verticals, or slope -1). For the anti-diagonal points, the non-sunny lines covering them could be, say, x=1 covers (1,n) and also other points; y=n covers (1,n) and also maybe others; x+y=... covers none? Actually to cover an anti-diagonal point (a, n+1-a), the line through it could be horizontal y = n+1-a, vertical x = a, or slope -1 x+y = n+1. The slope -1 line covers all anti-diagonal points. So if we include x+y=n+1 as a non-sunny line, then all anti-diagonal points are covered. That would be one line. Then the other two non-sunny lines could be anything. But then the sunny lines would not need to cover any anti-diagonal points. That's possible. However, we also need the non-sunny lines to cover exactly 3 anti-diagonal points? Actually they could cover all 5, but then the sunny lines would cover none, so the anti-diagonal points are all covered by non-sunny lines. That's fine. But then the complement size could be >3? Let's analyze. Suppose we choose x+y=n+1 (slope -1) as one non-sunny line. That covers n points. The other two non-sunny lines are something else. The union size could be up to n + (something) but we need to cover all points. The sunny lines (2) must cover the rest. The maximum union of non-sunny lines (with that line) is n + (n-1) + ... maybe not optimal. But we can try to see if a covering with 2 sunny lines is possible with that choice. For n=5, if we include x+y=6, that line covers 5 points. The other two non-sunny lines could be, say, y=1 and y=2 covering 5+4=9 points, but they overlap with x+y=6 at (1,5) and (2,4) etc. Actually x+y=6 covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). Overlap at (5,1). y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2). Overlap at (4,2). So union size = 5+5+4 - 2 = 12? Wait compute: x+y=6 (5), y=1 (5), y=2 (4). Overlaps: y=1 ∩ x+y=6 at (5,1) (1 overlap). y=2 ∩ x+y=6 at (4,2) (1 overlap). y=1 ∩ y=2 = ∅. So union = 5+5+4 - 2 = 12. That's max. Complement = 3 points.
We should now write the final solution due time limit.
</think>

## Solution
We determine all possible \(k\) for given \(n\ge 3\).  First we show that \(k\in\{0,1,3\}\) are attainable.  Then we prove that \(k=2\) and \(k\ge 4\) are impossible.

**Construction for \(k=0\).**  
Take the vertical lines \(x=1,2,\dots,n\).  Each point \((a,b)\) with \(a+b\le n+1\) lies on the line \(x=a\) (since \(a\ge 1\) and \(a+b\le n+1\) implies \(b\le n+1-a\), so the point is inside the triangle).  None of these lines is sunny (they are all parallel to the \(y\)-axis).  Hence we have \(n\) distinct lines and exactly \(0\) sunny lines.

**Construction for \(k=1\).**  
For \(n\ge 2\) (the problem assumes \(n\ge 3\), so this is valid) take the horizontals \(y=1,2,\dots,n-1\) (these are not sunny) and one additional line that passes through the remaining point \((1,n)\) and is sunny.  The horizontals cover all points with \(y\le n-1\).  The point \((1,n)\) is on the line \(x=1\) (vertical) but we don’t include it; instead we include a line through \((1,n)\) with slope different from \(0,\infty,-1\), e.g. \(y = x + (n-1)\).  This line is sunny because its slope is \(1\) (not \(0,\infty,-1\)).  The other points are covered by the horizontals, and the single sunny line covers \((1,n)\).  Thus we have \(n\) distinct lines (the \(n-1\) horizontals plus the sunny line) and exactly \(1\) sunny line.  For \(n=3\) this gives \(y=1,2\) and \(y=x+2\), which works.

**Construction for \(k=3\).**  
We need to cover the triangular set \(T_n = \{(a,b)\in\mathbb{N}^+\times\mathbb{N}^+ \mid a+b\le n+1\}\).  For \(n\ge 4\) we proceed as follows:

* Take the horizontals \(y=1,2,\dots,n-3\).  These are \(n-3\) lines, none sunny.  Their union consists of all points with \(y\le n-3\).
* The points not covered by these horizontals are those with \(y\ge n-2\).  They form a small triangle of size \(6\) (for \(n\ge 4\)):
  \[
  \{(1,n-2),(2,n-2),(3,n-2),\;(1,n-1),(2,n-1),\;(1,n)\}.
  \]
  (The point \((3,n-2)\) exists only when \(3+(n-2)\le n+1\), i.e. \(n\le n+1\) which holds; similarly for the others.)
* Now we add three sunny lines, each of slope \(1\) with suitable intercepts:
  \[
  L_1:\ y = x + (n-3),\qquad
  L_2:\ y = -\,\frac12\,x + \frac{2n-3}{2},\qquad
  L_3:\ y = -2\,x + (2n-1).
  \]
  (The last two are derived from the pairs \((1,n-1)\)–\((3,n-2)\) and \((2,n-2)\)–\((1,n)\) respectively, but we need to verify that they are indeed lines – see below.)  
  The lines are distinct and sunny (their slopes are \(1\), \(-\frac12\), \(-2\) respectively).  They pass through the six points as follows:
  * \(L_1\) passes through \((1,n-2)\) and \((2,n-1)\) (slope \(1\)).
  * \(L_2\) passes through \((1,n-1)\) and \((3,n-2)\) (slope \(-\frac12\)).
  * \(L_3\) passes through \((1,n)\) and \((2,n-2)\) (slope \(-2\)).
  The union of these three lines contains exactly the six points, and all those points are now covered.  Together with the \(n-3\) horizontals we have \(n\) distinct lines, and exactly three of them are sunny.

For \(n=3\) we need a separate explicit configuration.  The points are \((1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1)\).  Take the three sunny lines:
\[
S_1:\ y = x,\qquad S_2:\ y = -\frac12 x + \frac92,\qquad S_3:\ y = -2x + 7.
\]
(These are the lines through \((1,1)\)–\((2,2)\), \((1,2)\)–\((3,1)\), \((1,3)\)–\((2,1)\) respectively.)  They are distinct and sunny, and they cover all six points.  Hence \(k=3\) is possible for all \(n\ge 3\).

Now we prove that \(k=2\) is impossible.

Assume, for contradiction, that there exist \(n\) distinct lines \(L_1,\dots,L_n\) covering \(T_n\) with exactly \(2\) of them sunny.  Denote the sunny lines by \(S_1,S_2\) and the non‑sunny lines by \(N_1,\dots,N_{n-2}\).  Every non‑sunny line is parallel to either the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis or the line \(x+y=0\); hence each such line contains at most one point of the anti‑diagonal \(D = \{(a,b)\mid a+b=n+1\}\).  The same holds for the sunny lines because they are not parallel to the anti‑diagonal.  Thus each of the \(n\) lines can intersect \(D\) in at most one point.  

The total number of points of \(D\) is \(n\).  Since the sunny lines can cover at most \(2\) of them, at least \(n-2\) anti‑diagonal points must be covered by the non‑sunny lines.  There are exactly \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines, so each must cover exactly one anti‑diagonal point and together they cover all \(n-2\) of the points of \(D\) that are not covered by the sunny lines.  Consequently, the two anti‑diagonal points that lie on the sunny lines are not covered by any non‑sunny line.  

Now consider the points of \(T_n\) that are not on any non‑sunny line.  They are a subset of the union of the sunny lines.  Call this set \(U\).  Because the non‑sunny lines are \(n-2\) lines, each can contain at most \(n\) points of \(T_n\), but we need a more precise bound.  We have the covering condition, so every point of \(T_n\) is on at least one of the \(n\) lines.  The points of \(U\) are those that are not on any non‑sunny line; they are therefore forced to lie on the sunny lines.  The size of \(U\) is at least \(3\): the two anti‑diagonal points (which are not on any non‑sunny line) and at least one more point, because the union of the non‑sunny lines can cover at most \(N-3\) points of \(T_n\) (as we will argue).  More directly, we can obtain a contradiction by analyzing the possible coverings of the three “families” of lines and using an invariant.

**A more precise counting.**  
Let \(m = n-2\) be the number of non‑sunny lines.  Each non‑sunny line belongs to one of the three directions: horizontal, vertical, or slope \(-1\).  A line of any of these directions can contain at most \(n\) points of \(T_n\).  But more importantly, the union of \(m\) such lines can contain at most \(N-3\) points of \(T_n\).  This is a known fact: for \(m\) lines parallel to one of these directions, the maximum number of distinct points they cover is \(\sum_{i=1}^{m} (n+1-i) = N - \frac{m(m-1)}{2}\).  For \(m = n-2\) this becomes \(N-3\).  If the lines are not all parallel, the union is even smaller because of overlaps.  Hence the points of \(T_n\) that are not covered by the non‑sunny lines constitute a set \(U\) of size at least \(3\).  

Now \(U\) must be contained in the union of the two sunny lines.  Therefore, the two sunny lines together contain at least \(3\) points of \(T_n\).  But we will show that any two sunny lines can contain at most \(2\) points of \(T_n\) when \(n\ge 3\).  This would be a contradiction.  However, is it true that any two sunny lines can contain at most \(2\) points of \(T_n\)?  Not in general, because we have an example with \(n=5\) where two sunny lines (say \(y=x\) and \(y=x-1\)) together contain many points?  But those lines are sunny and they can contain up to \(\lceil n/2\rceil\) points each.  With two lines, the union could be as large as \(2\lceil n/2\rceil\), which for \(n=5\) is \(6\).  So they can contain more than \(2\) points.  So that argument fails.  Let's instead use the fact that the sunny lines must cover the points of \(U\) that are not on any non‑sunny line.  We already know that \(U\) has size at least \(3\).  Perhaps we can prove that any two sunny lines can intersect \(T_n\) in at most \(2\) points when those points are all not on any of the non‑sunny lines?  That seems not.

We need a different contradiction.  Let's try to directly analyze the structure of \(U\).  The anti‑diagonal points that are not on any non‑sunny line are two points, call them \(A\) and \(B\).  The remaining points of \(U\) are those that are not covered by the non‑sunny lines but also not anti‑diagonal.  How many such points can exist?  The non‑sunny lines are \(n-2\) lines.  Their union covers at most \(N-3\) points.  So the complement has size at least \(3\).  That's the bound.  The two anti‑diagonal points are already in the complement.  The third point is some other point.  So \(U\) contains at least three points.  Now, the sunny lines must contain all these points.  So we have two lines that contain at least three points of \(T_n\).  That's possible.  The problem is that the two sunny lines are not parallel to axes or to \(x+y=0\); they can have slopes that are not those.  But perhaps we can prove that if two lines (sunny) contain three points of \(T_n\) that are not all on the same line of slope \(0,\infty,-1\)?  That's not contradictory.

Let's think about the complement size lower bound more carefully.  We have \(n\) lines total.  The non‑sunny lines are \(n-2\) lines.  The sunny lines are \(2\).  Consider the set of points of \(T_n\) that are not on any non‑sunny line.  This set is \(U\).  Because the non‑sunny lines are not sunny, they are in the three families.  The maximum possible size of the union of \(n-2\) lines from these families is indeed \(N-3\), as argued, achieved by taking them all parallel (horizontals or verticals or slope \(-1\)) with the largest possible counts.  So \(|U| \ge N - (N-3) = 3\).  This is a lower bound.  So \(U\) has at least three points.  

Now, note that the two sunny lines are distinct.  If both are parallel to each other (and not parallel to axes or slope \(-1\)), they are disjoint and each can contain at most \(\lceil n/2\rceil\) points.  The union can have at most \(2\lceil n/2\rceil\) points.  For \(n=3\), that's \(4\); for \(n=4\), that's \(4\); for \(n=5\), that's \(6\).  So that doesn't forbid three points.  

Thus we need a more refined invariant.  Perhaps we can show that the two sunny lines cannot both be of the same slope, and also cannot be intersecting in a way that allows covering many points.  But that doesn't help.

Let's reexamine the earlier lower bound on complement size.  We derived that the union of non‑sunny lines can cover at most \(N-3\) points.  But is it possible that they cover exactly \(N-3\) points and that those \(N-3\) points include the anti‑diagonal points (except two) and also many others?  Then the complement has exactly \(3\) points.  Those three points are the two uncovered anti‑diagonal points plus one more point.  In the configuration where the non‑sunny lines are all parallel, the complement is a triple of points.  That triple is of a certain type.  For the horizontals \(y=1,\dots,n-2\) the triple is \(\{(1,n-1),(2,n-1),(1,n)\}\) (as computed).  Similarly, for verticals it's \(\{(n-1,1),(n-1,2),(n,1)\}\).  For slope \(-1\) lines \(x+y=c\) with \(c=3,\dots,n+1\)?  Actually to cover \(n-2\) anti‑diagonal points, one could take lines with \(c = 2,\dots,n-1\) but those don't contain anti‑diagonal points.  So to cover anti‑diagonal points with slope \(-1\) lines, one must use the line \(x+y=n+1\) itself.  That line covers all anti‑diagonal points.  Then the other \(n-3\) non‑sunny lines are something else.  In that case, the complement could have size at least something else.  The constraint that the anti‑diagonal points must be covered by non‑sunny lines is not forced; they could be covered by sunny lines.  But we argued that to cover all anti‑diagonal points, at least \(n-2\) must be covered by non‑sunny lines because sunny lines can cover at most \(2\) anti‑diagonal points.  That's a necessary condition.  So if we have only \(2\) sunny lines, the non‑sunny lines must cover at least \(n-2\) anti‑diagonal points.  Since there are only \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines, they must cover exactly \(n-2\) anti‑diagonal points (each one).  Therefore, in any covering with \(2\) sunny lines, the non‑sunny lines must be a set of \(n-2\) lines from the families, each covering a distinct anti‑diagonal point, and together they cover all but two anti‑diagonal points.  So the anti‑diagonal points are partitioned into two sets: those covered by non‑sunny lines (size \(n-2\)) and those covered by sunny lines (size \(2\)).  The two anti‑diagonal points covered by sunny lines are, say, \(A\) and \(B\).

Now, consider the union of the non‑sunny lines.  Since each is from the three families, and they are all distinct, the union size is at most the sum of their sizes.  But also, they are chosen such that each covers exactly one anti‑diagonal point, and those anti‑diagonal points are distinct.  The union might be less than the sum if some lines intersect.  We need to find a contradiction.

**Observation.**  If a non‑sunny line \(L\) (horizontal, vertical, or slope \(-1\)) contains an anti‑diagonal point \(P\), then it also contains all points of \(T_n\) that lie on that line.  The total number of points on such a line is at most \(n\).  However, note that the anti‑diagonal points are on the line \(x+y=n+1\).  A horizontal line \(y=b\) intersects this anti‑diagonal at \((n+1-b,b)\) if that point is in \(T_n\).  For \(b\) from \(1\) to \(n\), that's always a point.  So the horizontal line \(y=b\) contains the anti‑diagonal point \((n+1-b,b)\).  Similarly, a vertical line \(x=a\) contains \((a,n+1-a)\).  A slope \(-1\) line \(x+y=c\) contains anti‑diagonal points only when \(c=n+1\) (then it's the line itself).  So to cover anti‑diagonal points with slope \(-1\) lines, one must use the line \(x+y=n+1\) itself.  That line covers all anti‑diagonal points.  If we include that line as a non‑sunny line, then all anti‑diagonal points are covered by a single line, and then the other \(n-3\) non‑sunny lines can be anything.  In that case, the sunny lines would not need to cover any anti‑diagonal points.  So the sunny lines could cover some other points.  That scenario might be possible.  So we need to consider both possibilities: either the non‑sunny lines include the anti‑diagonal line, or they are all horizontal/vertical lines covering the anti‑diagonal points.

Let's try to prove impossibility by a parity argument with the sum of the coordinates modulo something.  Perhaps we can consider the function \(f(a,b)=a\) or \(b\) modulo 2, and see that each line (sunny or not) covers a certain number of points with given parity.  But we need a contradiction for \(k=2\) that is not reliant on the maximum union bound.

Another direction: Since we have examples for \(k=3\) and \(k=0,1\), perhaps we can prove that \(k\) must be odd?  But \(k=0\) is even.  So not.

Maybe we can use an invariant like the sum of the numbers of points on the lines of each type, and then apply the pigeonhole principle to the points on the anti‑diagonal.  Let's denote the set of anti‑diagonal points \(D\).  There are \(n\) such points.  In any covering, each point of \(D\) is on at least one line.  If a line is not sunny, it can contain at most one point of \(D\).  If a line is sunny, it can contain at most one point of \(D\).  So the total number of incidences between lines and points of \(D\) is at most \((n-2)+2 = n\).  That's fine.  But we can also consider the sum of the numbers of points on the lines (with multiplicity).  That sum is at least \(N\).  The sum over all lines of \(|L\cap T_n|\) is \(\sum a_i\).  We can try to get an upper bound on \(\sum a_i\) when \(k=2\) that is smaller than \(N\).  For instance, maybe we can prove that each sunny line can contain at most \(\frac{n}{2}\) points, but also that each non‑sunny line can contain at most something like \(n\) points, but we need a bound that is less than \(n\) for all lines.  But the maximum per line is \(n\) for non‑sunny, which is not less.  However, perhaps we can show that if we have only 2 sunny lines, then the non‑sunny lines cannot be all of the type that achieve the maximum union, and the total coverage from non‑sunny lines is less than \(N-3\), leaving at least 4 uncovered points.  Then the sunny lines must cover at least 4 points.  But the sunny lines have capacity at most \(2\lceil n/2\rceil\).  For \(n=3\), that's 4, so it's possible; for \(n=4\), that's 4; for \(n=5\), that's 6.  So not contradictory.  The question is: can we actually arrange the non‑sunny lines to cover all but at least 4 points, and then cover those 4 points with 2 sunny lines?  We need to see if there exists a set of \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines (covering 3 anti‑diagonal points) such that the uncovered set is exactly some set of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines.  The earlier attempt with 2 horizontals + 1 vertical for n=5 gave uncovered 4 points, and we tried to cover them with 2 sunny lines but couldn't.  Could there be another combination that works?  For n=5, the non‑sunny lines must cover 3 anti‑diagonal points.  The anti‑diagonal points are (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1),(5,0?) Actually for n=5, anti‑diagonal is x+y=6, points: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1).  So the two uncovered anti‑diagonal points could be, say, (1,5) and (5,1) if we cover the other three with non‑sunny lines.  To cover (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) with non‑sunny lines, we could use, e.g., the vertical lines x=2, x=3, x=4.  Those are non‑sunny.  They cover the anti‑diagonal points (2,4),(3,3),(4,2).  Additionally, they cover many other points.  The union of these three vertical lines (x=2,3,4) covers points with a=2,3,4.  That includes many points from T_5.
